CHIEFLY ABOVE BOOKS.
Some unemployed member of N.S.W.
Parliament might profitably devote a little
attention to Sydney Free Public Library.
In older days the management of this insti-
tution could only be described as scandalous.
No person is supposed - take away books
from the chief or reference branch, any more
than from the British Museum —not “ under
any circumstances’’says the rule; but this rule
never seems to have applied to influential
politicians or to friends of the trustees. One
now-dead politician is asserted to have ‘ ‘ bor-
rowed ” what books lie pleased, and returned
all but 200 or. so ; a former trustee’s little
collection of books is alleged, by persons
who inspected it after his death, to have con-
tained scores of volumes with the Library
stamp ; the Library funds were debited with
subscriptions for trustees’ ‘ ‘ private ” maga-
zines ; and so on.
* * * *
Latterly supervision has improved ; but it
still seems very lax. For example, up to
April, 1896, a valuable standard work,
Adams and Cunningham’s “The Swiss Con-
federation,” was missing from the Library ;
and its place on the shelf was occupied,
according to regulation, by a wooden dummy
labelled “Lent to Mr. -, July, 1894.”
Another important work on the Swiss Con-
stitution was labelled “ Lost by Mr. ”
(the same “ Mr. ”). After students
had been deprived of -the use of the former
volume for apparently nearly two years, it
was replaced—only the other day—by a new
copy; so possibly “Mr. lost “The
Swiss Confederation ” also. If he did, who
paid for the new copy? and is “Mr. ”
going to pay for the volume still missing ?
* * . * *
Again, it has not been uncommon with
readers to enquire at the Library for an
important book and be told it is “Out,” or
“Missing,” or “Lent to the Trustees.” (This,
mind you, is in the Reference Branch, where
uninfluentiaj people are—very properly—not
allowed to take a book out of the building.)
Sometimes the reply is “Gone to the
Binder ” —a reply which appears to have
convenient euphemistic possibilities ; ffince
an inspection last week (31st July, ’96)
showed that over a score of volumes were
marked “ Binder, 27/12/95 ; ” and it is pre-
sumed the Govt. Printing Office is not in
such a state of disorganisation that it takes
seven months to bind a book. On this
head, a comparison of Printing' Office
statistics with Public Library ditto might be
interesting.
» * * *
An enquiry last week for Lecky’s “Demo-
cracy and Liberty ” elicited that it was
“out.” A whole edition of Oliver Wendell
Holmes’s books was also ‘ ‘ out. ” Among the
other books “ out ” last week were McAdam’s
“ Lives of British Statesmen ” ; Brown’s
“Law and Lawyers Gordon’s “School
Electricity” ; Trautwine’s “ Civil Engineers
Pocket-book ” ; Voltaire’s 1 ‘ Visit to Eng-
land ” ; Silverton’s “ London and Provincial
Water Supply”; Colburn’s “Almanac for
3000 Years ” ; Schinelio’s and Hill’s “ Lexi-
con Manuale Grseco-Latinum ” ; Klaproth’s
“Asia Polyglotta ” ; Barrington’s “Familiar
Introduction to Heraldry” ; Morley’s “Life
of Richard Cobden,” vol. ii.; Brannt’s
“Practical Treatise on the Raw Materials
and the Rectification and Distillation of
Alcohol”; Bertillon’s “Instructions Sig-
naletiques pour Identification Anthropomfi-
trique ” and ‘‘ L’ Anthropometrique J udi-
ciaire k Paris en 1889”; “Extramural
Sepulture Report ” ; Chadwick’s “ Interment
in Towns ” ; Richmond’s ‘ ‘ Colours and
Colour-painting as applied to Lithography ”;
“The Parnellite Split”; “The Tenth
Census of the U.S.” ; Digby’s “History of
the Law of Real Properly ” ; Nicol’s “ Sta-
tistics ” ; “United States Immigration and
Emigration”; Keating’s “ History of Ire-
land” ; “ Ethics of Civilisation”; two volumes
of the “Pamphleteer”; and Dowden’s
“ Shakespeare : His Mind and Art.”
It is an intolerable perversion of the uses
and objects of the Free Public Library that
a class of privileged borrow ers should be per-
mitted to deprive the general student and
enquirer of the advantages of important
works such as these—even if they are re-
turned. But some of them are not returned.
The wooden dummies which replace them
are often marked boldly: “Missing.” The
Library dunno where they are, “ Binder ”
and “ Missing ” together totted up last week
about 100 Volumes • where are those volumes
now ?
*.* * *
It may be said, of course, that it is desir-
able that the Trustees should keep acquaint-
ance with books new and old ; and no one
would object to new books being withheld
from issue for a fortnight or so while the
Trustees glanced through them. But there
is no excuse for depriving general readers of
a book like Becky’s “ Democracy and
Liberty ” ior apparently an indefinite period
four months after publication, in order
to give a Trustee or his friend or
his friend’s friend the exclusive ad-
vantage of armchair perusal. It may
be said that it is desirable that extraordinary
privileges should be extended to known
students or specialists. Perhaps it is ; but
the books should never be taken out of the
library building.
* * * *
Or if they are, to meet the exigencies of
some unusual case, then a register open to
inspection by the public should be kept, and
the date of lending a book, name of person
to 'whom lent, date of return (if any), and
reasons for breach of rule should be regu-
larly entered. It will be noted that the
books named above as ‘ ‘ Missing ” are mostly
highly technical and expensive publications.
It is not the plebeian reader who walks off
with the public property : it is the profes-
sional or business person, the person who
finds it more convenient to address a note to
“ My dear ” than to pay a guinea out of
pocket-, and who frequently, it appears, finds
it inconvenient to return his borrowings.
And, in another particular, precaution is
needed. Anybody now may enter the Library
with a bag, fill it with books unobserved,
and walk out unquestioned. That risk is not
taken in other libraries, and it should not be
taken in Sydney when it can be avoided by
making people leave their bags at the door.
* * * *
The Cynicus Publishing Co., of London
(Davidson Bros., Sydney agents), sends The
Bulletin a collection of colored cartoons by
“ Cynicus.” “ Cynicus ”is a young Scotch-
man who, starting from nothing, has won
fame and very fair fortune by dint of hard
work and genius. Five years ago he swooped
down on-a fish-shop in a Drury-lane (Lon-
don) slum, converted it into a studio, and
forced the critics to praise him.' (As he is
blasphemous, and socialistic, and at war with
half the conventions, they didn’t in the
least wish to.) Now, the “first copies” of
his sketch-books sell up to 15 guineas each ;
he is an artist of leisure, able to winter in
Italy and go summer-caravanning in Eng-
HOME, SWEET HOME!
land ; he has been floated into a company ;
and one of the marvels of the Scotch village
he hails from is the class of. sixfcy-or-so
school-children who color the drawings he
sells.
*' * * *
The two specimens of “ Cynicus’s ” art
here given are familiar to but
they are characteristic. There is an economy
of line which you will find in Caldecott or
Phil May or half-a-dozen of the new school,
but there is in addition a power, a meaning,
a value, which, so far as the writer’s imper-
fect knowledge goes, only Hogarth and
Gilray among English, cartoonists have pre-
viousiy reached.
The essential de-
fect of so-called
Art is its defect
of these things.
“Art for Art’s
sake ” is the right
motto, doubtless ;
but, as frequent-
ly applied, it begs
the essential ques-
tion what Art is.
Art isn’t mere
repro duetion :
Nature lives. A
flat copy on canvas
has at best only
technical interest;
and Art isn’t
technique, though
technique is a
part of Art. In
all things living
there is Soul—
a real, vital
CAPITAL AND LABOUR.
Force—and Style alone isn’t Soul or Force.
At best, it is the husk ; the trappings ; the
setting of the gem.
* * * *
This reads as obscurely as an artist’s own
jargoning, but the meaning sought is that a
picture, to be Art even in the painter’s
limited sense, must make the same appeal as
Literature or Music. It must move you,
grip you, thrill you—either intellectually or
emotionally. (The emotional appeal is the
lower of the two, and the intellectual
appeal at its best includes the emo-
tional appeal.) Now, how many pic-
tures are painted before which you can
stand and say “Very pretty!” “Very
clever ! ” —and which straightway you can
pass from and forget utterly ? On a low esti-
mate, millions. And how many can you
stand before, as before Haydon’s canvases in
the Melbourne Gallery, and feel their message
being, as it were, burnt in—to remain whilst
this machine is to you ? Scores ? It is
doubtful.
• • * •
The claque and chatter of artists—there
never were such formalists—has surrounded
the standards of their art with a mist through
which it is hardly possible to see clearly.
But surely the criterion of a perfect picture
should be that of a perfect poem—it should
unite highest conception with highest ex-
pression ; the central idea should be para-
mount over all the intricacies of detail, which
yet should, to the least of them, be necessary
to its development and fulfilment; and the
final effect upon the mind should be an
inspiration, an exaltation, a nerving to strain
on and up for the realisation of the ideal thus
artistically portrayed.
* . * » *
The art of “ Cynicus ” does not by a good
deal reach this desideratum; yet it
has a meaning, an influence, a thought
at the back of it. Its essence is Idea,
not Form ; and the Form is effective
to drive home the Idea, which is sent
flying straight to the gold. There
is something of permanence in it,
coo; it does not deal with passing
moods and follies, but with vital themes
and persisting motives. The cuts
shown have lost much in losing size
and colour ; but enough is left to make
it fairly clear that the lesson of
Capital and Labour ” will stand
good as : long as - Capital and Labour
have a meaning : that “ Home, Sweet
Home ” has a moral (in the limited earthly
sense) almost universal: that, in a word,
you can’t know “ Cynicus ” without getting
stimulation, and suggestion, and maybe
wisdom which attainment is at least an
approximation to the Art we desire and
would adore.
* * *
When a doctor does not run too much
to invective against the rival practitioner,
(and even when he does) he can often
write things worth reading. What fol-
lows (as reported in the Atjst. Medical
Gazette) was 13r. Harhinson , s contribution
to Vic. Med. Association’s recent discussion
of the momentous question : Shall members
snioke at meetings '( (carried in the affirma-
tive for a six-months trial).
Dr. Harbinsoh said : A pipe, cigar, or cigarette,
smoked after meals, ensures rest and repose from
worrying reflections, thus acting undoubtedly in
a salutary manner. At the same time an increased
flow of saliva is produced by the exciting action of
the smoke on the nervous mechanism regulating
the salivary glands. The glands of the stomach,
being reflexly stimulated, pour out an additional
supply of gastric juice, thereby promoting, or at
least facilitating, digestion. Therefore,
For each a smoke I prescribe ; Ter in die et post eib.
It has a slight stimulative action on the nervous
system, an effect which does not appear if the
dose be excessive. This is not followed by any
depressing reaction. Soon the pulse becomes
quickened, and simultaneously there is an increase
in the frequency of the beats of the heart. When
smoked'by those accustomed to its use the leaf is
one of the most valuable sedatives for the restless-
ness of an overworked or worried brain. Some
smokers find it acts as a (“ nerve ”) stimulant to
mental work. In these cases the effect is prob-
ably not due to the nicotine itself, hut to the
stimulation of the sensory nerveS of the mouth.
These reflexly act on the vaso-motor centres, and
they in turn produce dilatation of the vessels of
the brain: the increased circulation in the cere-
brum, which necessarily follows, may prove the
vis a tergo to loosen ideas lying dormant there.
* * ’ * *
Of course (proceeded Dr. Harbinson) there are
individuals who are more Susceptible to the seda-
tive than to the stimulating properties of tobacco.
One of these happy mortals, in the club smoke-
room, suggested the following thoughts Very
delightful, indeed, must be the sensations of a
inan who, after having well dined, lights a cigar,
or meerschaum, and sinks into a cosy easy chair.
Step, gently in a few moments afterwards; enter
into conversation with him if you can. You’ll
find
“ The pipe, with solemn, interposing puff,
Ma es half a sentence at a time enough.”
Ah, what a careless, thoughtless, happy individual
he is. His dreamy languor; permeates you while
you watch. His physiognomy is a study in lazi-
ness. As you contemplate this lucky smoker you
realise that pleasure is good for man, and man for
pleasure made. You envy him while lie poses, first
in one position and then in another. Note his
legs’ abandon, after having been alternately flexed
and extended in every conceivable manner, with
apparently a fresh charm in each new posture.
And now the vest-buttons are tenderly released
from the fond eiiibraces of the button-holes. This
is the attitude par excellence. At last he has
reached the goal of contentment. Ah ! well it is
for him
“ Who, when again lh° night returns,
When again the taper burns,
Can affoid his tube to feed
With the fragruit Indian weed.
Happy thrice, and thrice again—
Happiest he of happy men ! ” r :
But don’t - ah, don’t ask him to move ; to even
revolve his eyes, if he seems not so disposed, will
be an exqui-ite trouble, lie feels offended if you
request him to pass you the-magazine he has just:
let slip from his fingers. He will hand it to you,
no doubt, rather than confess laziness, but lie
seems injured ; you have disturbed his siesta. He
may look obfuscated and obscured in the smoky
clouds, as he idly counts the curling rings. He
may read a novel, provided he commenced it
before the stage of enchantment. He might listen
to orthopaedic notes, as he removes-that foreign
body (the cigar) from the substance of his tongue,
or appreciate the cafe noir which he will sip if he
can manage to raise the cup to his mouth without:
disturbing the pose of his head. . Slowly the
fleecy clouds an- wafted upwards, carrying with
them thelittio worries, troubles, and annoyances of
life, and leaving behind them a sweet sense of
oblivion—“ ’Tis like the river which whoe’er doth
taste forgets his present griefs and sorrows past.
Then, as he builds-' castles in the ascending smoke,
the cigar-end falls from his fingers, his eyelids
droop, he sleeps:! ,
—and proceeds to dream the verses ascribed
to Lord Byron in Hamilton’s Collection. But
lie is happier asleep : so leave him.

BEFORE YOU
T. E A. F!
When you go out shopping, consider, think
of the money in your pocket, and take account
of the many stores whose various attractions
are laid before you. There is A, with temp-
tation in the form of a pretty show ; there is
B, whose wiles are innumerable ; and C,
who says that he is the man for your money ;
and so on, throughout a list as long as
George-street, each smiling dealer has some-
thing to say and to offer.
Please Pause!
Hearken not to the song of the syren, sing
she ever so sweetly ; heed not the voice of
the charmer, charm he never so wisely.
Your pocket’s concerned ; and there are
pirates about. As the poet pertinently puts
it: “ There be land-sharks and water-
sharks,” and it is for you to see that your
dealings are with none of these voracious
monsters. Take warning in time, for it will
be no use when the new taxes come round —
and the tax-gatherer won’t take no for an
answer—for you to plead : There would have
been enough and to spare had I been wise
enough to

Hards of the Backblocks.
HOOSHTAH!
[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
Don’t you hear the lusty roarin’ of the crazy camel-
cow ?
Ahmed Khan’s a-haulin’ nose-pegs an’ the beast
has got to bow.
Hear him shriek the mandate “ Hooshtah ! ” —
word that is a camel’s “ must
Hooshtah! means: “You cursed beast, you, get
your belly in the dust !
For, by God, we’ve got to load you for the dusty,
hopeless track,
And you’ll go down if we fire you from your hooks
up to your back.
Go down, you blasted, stinkin’ brute; drop on
your thorax pad,
Or we’ll give you samphire firing and with other
woods as bad.
So Hooshtah ! Hooshtah !
Drop your curling nostril and get down upon the
pad,
Hooshtah / Hooshtah !
Or by the Lord that isn’t here we’ll fire your
rump, my lad.
Once we met an Afghan washing of his feet at
Gaudy Rocks,
And my mate with a revolver gave that Afghan
sundry shocks.
Fancy beasts like that a-spoilin’ gracious gnamma-
holes and soaks,
While we whites go sockless for a month—for
such Asiatic blokes !
An’ these stinkin’ Kurrachee-men claim the only
water near,
And when you enter protests they just drop the
silent tear :
So at Gaudy Rocks, when Mr. Nhubby Bhuks
had washed his feet,
My Matey dropped him sudden (after givin’
warning meet) . • ■_
And the bullet —which was conical—said “ Hoosh-
tah ! ” unto Bhuks,
And his countrymen removed him in assorted
kinds of trucks.
So Hooshtah! Hooshtah l
Bhuks washed ’em in the soak,
And my matey’s trigger broke,
And Hooshtah!
I was once dead gone—my oath, yes ! —on a young
North Melb’ne girl,
But she wouldn’t have no tradesmen —an’ my hair
got out o’ curl;
She would only have a toff, she useter say down
at her shop !
And when a clerk came on the beat, I got the
sudden drop.
So I said my heart was broken an’ I up an’ stowed
away
On a West Australian hooker what was leavin’
Hobson’s Bay,
An’ I struck it at Dead Finish ! An’ she’s Mrs.
Lawyer’s-clerk,
An’ they hunt for something eatable from morning
till the dark
To feed three cormorantic kids—themselves they
don’t much count —
An’ I drink no less than Pommer-OY an’ Hidesic
an’ Lay-Mount—
So Hooshtah ! Hooshtah !
I’ve got her by the nose-peg—I can buy a ’ouse
an’ park ;
She’s starvin’ with her clerk, from the dawn until
the dark,
And Hooshtah I
Once when I was with the push at Richmond
Sunday nights,
Larkin’ round the churches, throwin’ stones an’
seein’ sights—
Then the jay what takes the plate around an’
snipes the people’s “trays ”
’Ud leave his prayers now an’ then an’ knock us
gutter-ways.
Now he says, “ Oh, Mr. Dooley, will you join the
choir at tea ?
For the church there’s a collection, an’ a sermon,
an’ a glee.”
Then I pulls a fatty purse out, an’ I shows him
trays an’ quids—
“Narks yer,” says I, “don’t it, matey? You
what used to boot the kids ! ”
He don’t seem to take no notice, but he says—
“ The ’oly fold
Is as stiff as forty crutches—give us some Dead
Finish gold !
We are slaves to God (an’ Dooley—while he’s got
a extry shirt)
If you’d like some pious exercise, why, tread us in
the dirt ! ”
So Hooshtah ! Hooshtah !
When you’ve beans the world can only kiss ; when
you’re broke—oh, can’t it bite !
But I’ve got it strapped alright
And I hold its nose-line tight,
And Hooshtah !
RANDOLPH BEDFORD.
Rough-and-Tumble, W.A.

THE GOD-FEARING N.S.W. MINISTEV. —A VALUABLE SUGGESTION.
Now that Sydney Zoo is to be opened on Sunday there should be instituted a few exhibitions of Moral Lion-Taming—or something else in the
Pleasant Sunday Afternoon line. Perhaps the God-fearing Minister Jake Garrard will volunteer?

expressly reserved by the publishers.

whether in the form of Political, Social, or other Articles,
Verse, Short Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Aus-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in
length, or, say, 3000 words, are specially acceptable),
Paragraphs, Letters, or Newspaper-Clippings. All
communications tvill be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. when
the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But we
desire those sending MS. to distinctly understand that
we are not responsible for its preservation or transmission.

SATURDAY, AUGUST 15, 1896. -
The Privy Council Trouble.
There are now three candidates in the field
for the billet of Australian Judge on the
Judicial Committee of the British Privy
Council— Way, of South Australia, Wil-
liams, of Victoria, and Windeyer, of New
South Wales. So far as present appearances
go, the appointment depends on a scramble
among these three, and the six Governments
of Australia and Tasmania are much agitated
among the conflicting claims of the candi-
dates. And, so far, not one Government—
not even the democratic Kingston Ministry
at Adelaide, nor the Turner Cabinet of Vic-
toria, whose principal characteristics have
hitherto been plain horse-sense and a hatred
of unnecessary frill and gaudiness—has risen
to point out the most important aspect of
the case : that Australia has no use for either
Windeyer, Way nor Williams, nor any
other Australian Judge on the Privy Council,
nor for any Privy Council whatever, and
that the whole business is mere waste and
vanity and useless accumulation of law-costs.
Provincial jealousies are so rampant in Aus-
tralia that every Government is willing and
eager to perpetuate the curse of a foreign
court of appeal, provided it can get its own
nominee appointed thereon. Why there
should be this great desire to grab at the
positionis difficult to understand. Probablyit
hasn’t yet occurred to the public that New
South Wales won’t be enriched by a single
penny if Windeyer is appointed, and if
Williams gets the billet Victoria won’t be
one straw the better for it, and that it
wouldn’t advantage South Australia an iota
if its own Sammy Way sat on the Privy
Council from now till the day of judgment.
♦ * * *
The idea of appointing an Australian Judge
arose from the fact that the Privy Council,
in the main, knows nothing of Australian
law. Therefore, the final court of appeal
for this country is a tribunal which, in very
many cases, is blankly ignorant of the law it
is administering. It is proposed to remedy
this by appointing an Australian Judge to
hear Australian appeals, on the off-chance
that he may know something of the law he
is administering. As each of the seven pro-
vinces, however, has a legal system almost
entirely its own, even the Australian Judge
won’t necessarily know much. If Samuel
Way gets the billet he will doubtless be an
authority on appeals from South Australia, and
on matters specially relating to Westralia,
Victoria, Tasmania, N.S. Wales, Queens-
land, and Maoriland he can’t be any more
useless than any educated layman might be.
Therefore, the improved situation which it is
proposed to bring about seems to be very
little better than the present one, and the
present one is wholly ridiculous, inasmuch
as it often consists of a court which
doesn’t know the law of the case re-
voking the decisions of one that does.
There seems to be absolutely no reason why
any province should contribute towards the
salary of the new Judge, except the one
which he belongs to. The others certainly
receive no advantage from his appointment.
Whether the one to which he belongs receives
any advantage doesn’t appear, but it gets the
honor, if there is any, and possibly that
counts for something.
♦ * * *
This, however, is by no means the most
important aspect of the case. If Way or
Williams or Windeyer is to form a final
Court of Appeal on Australian matters, no
one has yet explained what possible end is
served by his sitting in London instead of in
Australia. His knowledge of Australian law
wouldn’t be improved but rather deteriorated
by sending him 16,000 miles away from the
place where new Australian law is being
made, so as to make it as difficult as possible
for him to keep pace with legal developments.
He would be half the globe removed from the
Courts whose actions he had to revise. Just
now we have the Judge and the cases he
will have to decide both on the spot, and it
is deliberately proposed to send the Judge
to about the farthest-away place on earth,
and send the cases after him, and let them
keep going after him or his successors for
ever, to the great inconvenience of all parties.
If Samuel Way is fit to finally decide an
Australian appeal-case in England, he is
equally fit to decide it here, and the same
logic applies to every other candidate for the
office. The sending of Way to do the work
in England only means that for every appeal
heard there will be about six months’
needless delay; and, perhaps, £5OO or
£lOOO of needless expense ; and vast
injustice, because the poor litigant will,
by the delay and expense, be deprived
of his appeal altogether ; and more misery
and trouble and hope deferred than anyone
can estimate. And the whole business is
just as foolish as if an Australian citizen,
when he wanted his hair cut, had to send the
barber to England, and then go there after
him and have his hair abbreviated in London,
when it might have been done quite as well
by the same man on the spot.
* * * ♦
The stereotyped argument advanced in de-
fence of the present system consists of a
large and gorgeous statement to the effect
that no British subject can be justly deprived
of the privilege of carrying his appeal to the
highest court of the Empire. This vast and
pompous remark is much less beautiful, how-
ever, than it looks. The British subject is
already debarred from carrying his case to
the highest court of the Empire if it con-
cerns a matter below £5OO in value. That
knocks out about 99 per cent, of civil liti
gants in one act. He is further debarred if
he can’t find security for costs and if he can’t
stand the enormous expense and heart-
breaking delays of the business, and that
shuts the door of the highest court of appeal
to perhaps half the remainder. On a low
estimate, the expense and worry of the
system do actually deprive probably 995
people out of every 1000 of the glorious
privilege aforesaid, and, under these circum-
stance, the wild and whirling observations
about the rights of a British subject are the
veriest drivel. Moreover, the right of every
British subject to carry his case to the
highest court in ‘ Victoria’s dominions is
only another name for the wrong of
every British subject to be dragged
to the farthest ends of the earth in
resisting an unjust claim, and to be perse-
cuted at enormous loss and inconvenience by
any enemy who has money enough to pay
expenses with. And as the great majority
of Privy Council appeals are dismissed it
follows that that institution acts much oftener
(indirectly) as an engine of oppression than
as a means of obtaining justice. Moreover,
even in those cases where it reverses the
decisions of other courts there is no possible
evidence that it is doing justice ; quite as
likely as not it is committing a mistake and
doing a gross wrong, therefore its value is
wholly problematical. In any case, if it is a
valuable privilege to have the right of appeal
to the highest court in the empire, and Way
or Williams or Windeyer is to be that
court, or a part of it, then either of them
might as well be that court in Australia as
in England. And if there is any special
advantage to a grovelling community in the
right to carry its case to an Imperial tribunal,
then let Way or either of the other men be
the same Imperial tribunal in Australia as
he would be in London, and wear the same
scarlet clothes with balloon sleeves, or what-
ever they are, and call himself by the same
title, whatever it may be, and the thing is
done.
* * * *
What is really wanted is not more law, or
law that is more mathematically accurate, or
that can split a hair to a greater nicety ; it is
law that is cheap, prompt and accessible.
The civil law, as it now stands, admits the
possibility ©f four or five successive trials or
appeals in Australia and a final appeal to the
British Privy Council in addition. The Kil-
patrick case, which has dragged its slow
length along in Melbourne for over two
years, is a recent and conspicuous evidence
of this state of things. The law asserts that
it prolongs itself in this dreadful fashion in
its desperate desire to do absolute and com-
plete justice, but it would be much better
for all parties if it distributed justice with
more speed, even if some of the accuracy had
to be sacrificed. When a just verdict for,
say, £5OO is given after all the possibilities
of hearing and re-hearing are exhausted, it
may easily cost the winner £lOOO to win it
and the loser twice that amount to lose it,
and it would have been much cheaper for all
parties save the legal brigade if a hard and
fast line were drawn at one hearing and one
appeal to a local Court. Even if the result of
this limitation were sometimes an unjust ver-
dict, that would almost always be cheaper than
a mathematically just one arrived at under the
present system. Moreover, the policy which
permits of five or six separate hearings does
not by any means make justice any surer ; it
makes nothing surer except costs. A new
trial or an extra appeal is quite as likely to
upset a just verdict as an unjust one, and the
Privy Council probably upsets as many good
decisions as bad ones, and confirms as many
bad decisions as good ones. And if a liti-
gant, who has already, in all probability,
had three hearings, would be grievously
wronged as alleged if he were deprived of a
fourth appeal to the Privy Council, then
he is equally wronged in not having some
court, or some three or four courts, to which
he can appeal after he leaves the Privy
Council. And if it would be a grievance if
his right of appeal ended in an Australian
court, it is an equal grievance that it does
end in a British one, and doesn’t go on to
some other court 10,000 miles away in a new
direction. In short, if it is a grievance that
the right of appeal should end in one place,
it is an equally good grievance that it should
end in any other place, or in any place
whatsoever.
* * * *
Obviously, however, the line must be
drawn somewhere. And it must be a purely
arbitrary line, drawn as a mere matter of
convenience and not as a matter of abstract
justice, for no one knows where abso-
lute justice is located, and if 40 suc-
cessive appeals were allowed it is quite
as probable that the last one would
do a gross injustice by reversing the
just decision which had preceded it as
that it would undo a wrong previously com-
mitted. Therefore, as a matter of expedi-
ence, The Bulletin urges the establishment
of a Federal Australian court of final appeal,
composed of one Judge from each province ;
and the entire abolition of this country’s
connection with the British Privy Council.
An Australian tribunal would be rea-
sonably accessible, which the Privy Council
isn’t. It would be moderately cheap,
which the aforesaid Council never has
been and never can be. It would be
respectably prompt in its decisions, because
it would save the time wasted in humping
the case round the world and back again.
It would know Australian law, which the
present Privy Council doesn’t. Because
the present Privy Council doesn’t know Aus-
tralian law it is proposed to appoint an
Australian Judge upon it to hear Australian
cases, and the same man would hear cases in
the Federal court, so the situation would be
the same—barring the frill and expense and
delay. The costs would go to Australian
lawyers, and the system would make one
absentee the less, and it would do something
to help along the Federal movement, and it
wouldn’t materially damage the “ silken
bonds ” in any way The Bulletin knows of
—the present system of dragging infuriated
litigants at a great expense into English law-
courts probably does the “bonds” a lot
more harm than good. And if Australia
must have a British Privy Council to appeal
to, then this Court can be a British Privy
Council—or a football, or a haggis, or what-
ever other name is wanted. And, against all
this, there is no reason on the other side that
this paper has heard of, nor has it heard of
anybody who has heard of one. The dull
conservatism of the law and lawyers and the
apathy of other people are the only visible
causes why the present system has endured
so long.
“The Burns Myth.”
Dear Bulletin, —Apart from the opinion of
Carlyle and Scotchmen generally, why was not
Burns a great poet per se ? The case does not
stand proved that he is only a great Scotch poet.
Thousands of Lowland Saxons, who hate, Gaelic
like poison, love and admire Burns. And Gaelic
is a sair dose to gulp down, even when strongly
diluted with whisky. The point that all Scottish
poetry is Celtic is, however, true. The Celtic
race, which traces its source back about 1000 years
before Christ to a locality in Turkey, includes the
Gaelic. Brittany, Wales, Isle of Man, Highlands
of Scotland, West of Ireland are its strongholds—
and Ayr was once the seat of one of the earliest
known native rulers of the Celts. Consequently
all Scotchmen are Turks, and that is perhaps why
Burns was so fond of the women.
It is true that Burns produced no great epic, no
classic drama. But does the production of great
epics and classic dramas alone constitute greatness
in poetry ? Sometimes there is greatness in the
poetic atmosphere of the thought, in the note that
runs through the work. Burns was nothing if not
the poet of Democracy, and it is the Democratic
note expressed lyrically that makes him great.
His eternal theme is the dignity of labor, the
brotherhood of man. He is direct, potential,
tender, humorous, stirring the deepest chords. He
was ahead of his time, and we are not abreast of
him yet. Like Heine, but without Heine’s cul-
ture, and far below the German in intellectual
strength, he fought, a brave soldier in the cause of
Humanity.
At his best his thought is robust, world-embrac-
ing, scintillating with wit; his lyrics of the purest;
his fire not surpassed by any poet in the English
and Celtic anthology. In spontaneity he was the
equal of Byron, but with saner, more catholic,
truer insight. Wordsworth was not above him as
a nature poet, nor Shelley as a lyric. In pure
poetic strength and originality, Keats, Shelley,
and even Tennyson are but beautiful children
beside him —weavers of wondrous words, makers
of delicious music, but charged with no electric
message to thrill the hearts of men. ,
* * * *
“No one,” says Laing, in his essay on thecreeds
of great poets, “equals Burns in the keenness of
insight with which he looks through tbe outer
husks and habiliments of things to their real
essence.” Carlyle’s clothes philosophy in “Sartor
Resartus ” is but a sermon on the text :
The rank is but the guinea stamp,
• The man’s the gold for a’ that.
* * *
Of course, Burns wrote an intolerable deal of
trash, but so did Tennyson. And to compare him
to Tupper is enough to make Saint-Beuve turn in
his grave.
Anyhow, how much of the Victorian poetry is
going to live at the end of the twenty-second cen-
tury ? It is only about a thousandth part of what a
poet writes that really lives, becomes incorporated
with the language and crystallised into the thought
of the world. And Burns has just as good, if not
a better, chance than Tennyson, Browning, and
Swinburne. And if (in Matthew Arnold’s words
on Goethe) Burns cannot be said “ to have seen
Life steadily and seen it whole,” he saw with a
piercing vision everything in life that related to
mankind. His intensity, his grasp, and his splen-
did enthusiasm for Humanity—fighting grimly to
a higher goal—make him of more use to the world
than all the cold, universal culture of the Sage of
Weimar.
Perhaps the best words ever said on Burns are
by the English poet, William Watson, himself a
lyric poet not inferior to Keats. I give a few dis-
jointed stanzas from a long poem :
He came when poets had forgot
How rich and strange the human lot;
How warm the tints of Life ; how hot
Are Love and Hate ;
And what makes Truth divine, and what
Makes Manhood great.
For, ’mid an age of dust and dearth,
Once more had bloomed immortal worth.
There, in the strong, splenetic north,
The Spring began—
A mighty mother had brought forth
A mighty man.
And though thrice statelier names decay,
His own can wither not away
While plighted lass and lad shall stray
Among the broom,
Where evening touches glen and brae
With rosy gloom ;
While nations see in holy trance
That vision of the world’s advance,
Which glorified his countenance
When from afar
He hailed the Hope that shot o’er France
Its crimson star ;
While, plumed for flight, the soul deplores
The cage that foils the wing that soars;
And while, through adamantine doors
In dreams flung wide,
We hear resound, on mortal shores,
The immortal tide.
Toowoomba, Q. Anglo-Australian.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Fruit Export Business.
The N.S. Wales Board of Exports, an institu-
tion created by the present Foreign- trade
Ministry, proposes to find a market for N.S. W.
produce in England, and in order to create a
trade it is going to buy and export 3000 cases
of fruit. There are various quaint aspects
about this new departure. New South Wales
is one of the finest fruit countries on earth, yet
at present it hardly grows enough fruit for its
own consumption. Of what it does grow a
large proportion goes to waste, because almost
the only market—Sydney—is flooded, under
the Foreign-trade regime, with immense quan-
tities of imported produce from all manner of
markets, near and far. Somehow, under Free-
trade, the local grower doesn’t contrive to hold
his own even in his own market against the
far-away outlanders. The principles of the
Reid Government don’t permit it to take any
steps to secure the N.S. W. grower control of
his own market, but they do allow it to take
special measures to enable him to scramble for
a market 16,000 miles away. The department
is to buy the fruit and pay railway and sea
freights and all other expenses to London,
and sell the goods there for what they will
fetch. As the grower, in many instances, can’t
get enough for his fruit to pay expenses to
Sydney, let alone to London, the assumption is
that the transaction will be conducted at a
heavy loss, which will be charged to the general
taxation. Therefore, it appears that, though
there is great objection to taxing the people by
Protection in a way which will not only bring
money to the revenue but will secure the
N.S.W. fruit-grower his own market, there is
no objection whatever to taxing them in a way
which will take money out of the revenue, and
will leave the fruit-person as hopelessly unable
to compete in his own market as he was before.
If the experiment proves that England will buy
N.S.W. fruit, then it is assumed that the
N.S.W. grower will proceed to ship it to
London - on his own account, and grow rich on
the trade that will ensue. He will do this,
partly because, under the discouragement of
Freetrade, he doesn’t grow enough to supply
local demands ; and partly because, as he can’t
send it the short distance to Sydney cheaply
enough to oust the far-off outsider and get con-
trol of the Sydney market, the natural thing
to do is to send it 16,000 miles further. The
Freetrade policy is absolutely averse to assist-
ing or subsidising any industry—but that, ap-
parently, only applies to subsidising it in the
local market. So the fruit in question will be
trained to Sydney, and then laboriously humped
to the ship’s-side, past the shops where the Fiji
bananas and the apples, pears, and plums from
Tasmania and Victoria and Maoriland, and
the Italian lemons, and the English jams,
and the dried fruits from ’Murka are for
sale. And then the ship will carry it “ home,”
passing on its way other ships bringing similar
fruit for sale in Sydney. And when N.S.W.
thus exports, say, ,£2OOO worth of fruit a dis-
tance of 16,000 miles, and imports £6OOO worth


of the same sort from other great distances, the
aggregate trade will be swelled by £BOOO, and
heavy remarks will be made about the way
things are looking up under the foreign shoddy
regime. Whereas under Protection it is pro-
bable the £2OOO worth wouldn’t be exported at
the public expense, and the £6OOO wouldn’t be
imported for the local grower would supply the
market, and the country would save the differ-
ence of £4OOO, and the statistics would show
that trade had diminished by £Booo—which
circumstance would clearly prove the decad-
ence which necessarily follows on a Protective
policy.
Seddonism in Maoriland.
The M.L. Government reputation for honest
finance, which hasn’t been in any too good
health of late, has practically gone bung, as
shown by Premier Seddon’s own admissions in
his financial statement. According to the
cynical candour of Richard of Kumara,
Stout’s story about the famous sinking-fund
swindle is all true ; unlike Ward, who shuffled
over the matter and gave evasive answers,
Digger Dick acknowledges it quite cheerfully,
and says the job is still going on, and wants to
know why anybody should object to it. The
sinking-fund matter is an ancient, smellful
affair which is difficult to sort out, therefore
most people let it slide ; the average individual
could understand and applaud when Digger
Dick offered to whirl a superfluous pick and
shovel in the caved-in Brunner mine, but the
obscure ways of finance are beyond him.
Many years ago, when Maoriland was more
sanguine than it is now, it floated loans with a
sinking-fund attached to each —an annual pay-
ment which was to accumulate into a sum large
enough to pay off the debt when it fell due. It did
accumulate for some time. Then Premier Stout
and his hard-up Ministry struck an idea ; the
payments to the sinking-fund couldn’t legally
be suspended, but an Act was passed authorising
the Government to borrow each year and put
into the revenue as much money as was paid
out of the revenue that year for sinking-fund
purposes. This in itself didn’t matter much ;
it merely put Maoriland in the position of
having no sinking-fund at all, which is also the
position of New South Wales, Victoria, and
some other provinces. But the Act, like most
Acts drawn up by lawyers, was a badly-con-
structed and leaky affair, and, instead of speci-
fying that the Government might only borrow
an amount equal to what it actually paid into
the sinking-fund, it gave a large, loose authority
to grab sinking-funds in general. The dodgy
Treasurer Ward saw the weak spot, and when he
was dreadfully hard-up for a surplus he availed
himself of it, and spent the rest of his political
life refusing to furnish information on the sub-
ject. In Maoriland, the central Government
finances for many of the local bodies ; it borrows
money, and re-lends it to them, and the local
bodies make annual payments into a sinking-
fund to liquidate the debt. Treasurer Ward
struck the great idea of borrowing these funds
also, and now, if the general revenue pays, say,
£50,000 into the State sic king-funds and the
local bodies pay £70,000 into their own sinking-
funds in a year, the Government borrows
£120,000 and puts it into the ordinary revenue,
and has a real good time with the proceeds.
Therefore, so far as the £70,000 is concerned,
it amounts to the same as if the Government
borrowed it in London and put it into revenue
at once. The local body has raised, say,
£IOO,OOO from the British money-lender,
through its agent, the Government, and it pays
the loan back by instalments to the Government
as agent for the British money-lender ; and the
Seddon Ministry, to all intents and purposes,
acts as the dishonest trustee in the business,
and annexes the money, and calls it revenue,
and makes the original liability a permanent
addition to the public debt. Seddon stands
fast on the plea that this disgraceful proceeding
is perfectly legal, just as Reid stands on the
legality of the finance which produced his
faked surplus, and in each case the statement is
correct; both matters are legal according to a
foolish and muddled law which the Legislature
passed without realising its meaning, and which
an unscrupulous Minister has taken advantage
of to the uttermost. The political morals of the
average Australasian Treasurer are not up to
much. His main idea is to make a surplus by
any dodge which isn’t distinctly illegal, or by
crawling through any hole which Parliament
has omitted to stop up. And then he sometimes
denies that he has done it, and at other times,
as in the case of Digger Dick, he simply says
that he is going to keep on doing it, and that,
as long as his party-majority stands by him, his
critics may go to Tophet, and that, while the
proceeding is legal, by accident or otherwise,
its political morality doesn’t signify.
The Pleasant Sunday Afternoon and
the Nude Model.
Ed. Bulletin, —The thanks of the community
„re due to last week’s Sydney “ Pleasant Sun-
day Afternoon” meeting. These “Pleasant
Sunday Afternoons,” by the way, are not half
so well patronised as they deserve to be. Pro-
perly viewed, there is no more interesting and
instructive way for “men only ” to spend an
hour and a-half than to come and listen to half-
a-dozen well-selected saints discussing the
social evil, the age of consent, the impropriety
of the nude, and so on. The only place
where I remember to have experienced exactly
the same sensations was in Dupont-street,
San Francisco, where picked specimens of all
the women on the globe—French, English,
Scotch, Negro, Chinese, Jap., German, Russian,
and Mongrel —sit each in her little shop, behind
her little (jrille, and beckon to the strange
voung man after the fashion so graphically
described by our old friend Solomon. Perhaps
the Dupont-street exhibition is more various;
but for real, stimulating, cayenne-peppery
unction the “Pleasant Sunday Afternoon”
is way up top But to return to last
Sunday’s Afternoon, at which forcible re-
ference w'as made to the apathy of Govern-
ments in matters of social purity. While
one member of the Goo-fearing Ministry gets
his red shirt off at a Salvation Army meeting,
and another lay-preaches in the back-blocks on
the necessity for obtaining the Joy, the moral
and artistic sense of the youth of Sydney is
being corrupted by the exhibition of cheap
female models at the Technical College. The
Pleasant Sunday Afternoon was told that on at
least one occasion a model was naked, and
it was rightly declared that this is one of the
things w’hich the Govt, should Put Down.
There is no doubt about it. Contemplation of
the average nude female form is destructive of
all the ideals which the community should
prize. I do not know what the Pleasant Sun-
day meeting’s experience has been, but I have
seen some hundreds of nude females of
all countries and ages, and I assure
you, Mr. Editor, that there was only
one of them worth looking at twice, and
she was a black-gin. More, I assert that
not one woman in ten thousand is fit to be seen
without her clothes on. The average woman is
lop-sided, short-legged, gross-curved, bulgy, and
unshapely ; and the exhibition of her for alleged
artistic purposes should be prohibited by
legislative enactment. I am thoroughly in
accord with the Pleasant Sunday meeting.
If Govt, wishes Technical College pupils to
study the female form, it should procure, not a
chance model hired “ for a few shillings,” but
the very best that money can buy. I would
cheerfully leave her selection from the mass of
candidates to a committee of the whole of
N.S.W. GoD-fearing Ministry, and the same
authority should be empowered to inspect her
from time to time to see that she did not
deteriorate from the high standard which
gentlemen of their attainments would naturally
fix. Thus only can we secure the end which
the Pleasant Sunday Afternoon yearns for, and
save the artistic instincts of our young men
and women from becoming hopelessly depraved.
—B.B.
Sweating the Civil Servant.
G. Read Murphy, a Victorian clerk of
courts, wrote in his own time a pamphlet
for a Melbourne dentist, and, failing to get
his hire, sued the dentist and got a verdict.
Thereupon, owing to pressure from some obscure
quarter, Vic. Law' Department has written to
Murphy telling him he must not do it again,
and drawing his attention—and through him
that of all civil servants —to section 116 of the
Public Service Act, 1890, the marginal note to
which runs—“ No officer in the Public Service
to engage in duties unconnected with his office.”
Right here the Law Department writes itself
down an ass. Murphy made a single contract
and carried it out, and the department on the
strength of that note threatens him. But
the marginal note is not law' at all, and
furthermore, the said note does not correctly
summarise the section, for throughout the
section continuity is implied, the phrases run-
ning “ accept or continue to hold any paid
office,” “engage or continue in the private
practice of any profession,” “ accept or con-
tinue to hold any municipal or corporation
office.” The intention of the section was evi-
dently to prevent the running of a private and
a public business at the same time in such a way
that public business w'ould be interfered with.
Thus a railway stationmaster must not likewise
be a regular grocer or butcher, but he may sell
a bag of flour or half a sheep to the man next
door. And, particularly, it could scarcely be
seriously intended that a civil-servant with
some intellect should not use that intellect for
his own purpose in his own time. Must he leave
his brain behind him daily in the red-tape and
sealing-wax department, and go home imbecile
and vacuous ? And, particularly, must literary
w'ork be forbidden to servants of the State ?
Were such the case, most of Marcus Clarke’s
best fiction w'ould never have seen the light;
A. W. Howitt’s splendid anthropological labors
would have been lost to Australia; so w'ould
George Gordon M'Crae’s verse, and much of
Henry Kendall’s, and Philip J. Holds-
worth’s, and Brunton Stephens’, and Tom
Bracken’s not to mention many inferior
lights. So, too, some of the best leaders
and articles in the Australian press w'ould
have remained unborn in Australia. Take
similarly a few British names Robert
Burns, Charles Lamb, Alfred Austin, Ed-
mund Gosse, Austin Dobson, Anthony Trol-
lope, General Wolseley, Admiral Clements
Markham, and last, and in many w'ays greatest,
Thomas Henry Huxley. Carried out logically,
then Premier Turner, while drawing salary of
office, should not plead in court; Alfred
Deakin should not write for the Age, nor
Murray Smith for the Argus ; laughing Pea-
cock should not legal-manage; nor Percy
Chirnside “squat”; nor Hancock secretary
the Typographical Union ; nor Bromley' japan.
If the State demands every man’s total labor
for a State salary, then M’s.L.A. are the worst
offenders of all. Furthermore, if this be so,
then the State, by demanding 24 hours’ use of
a man’s intellect, is the worst sweater that
could possibly be.
A Bill Drafted by the New Woman.
The Maoriland National Council of Women has
drafted a Bill—which it wants to have rushed
through Parliament at once—for the abolition
of a lot of things. The three crucial clauses are
as follows :
2. Coverture both as to person and property, and all
the incidents thereof, as known and established by law in
the colony of New Zealand, shall be and are hereby
abolished ; and all the laws and customs in force in the
said colony relating to coverture are hereby repealed.
3. From and after the passing of this Act every man and
every woman already married, or who may marry, shall in
all their economic relations be, and be deemed to be, sepa-
rate persons in law and in equity as to all property, means
and estate which shall belong to each before or at mar-
riage, or which either may succeed to, acquire or earn
during marriage ; provided always that every man and
every woman before marriage may enter into such lawful
contracts as they judge suitable to provide for the main-
tenance of the household and the management of their
domestic concerns as freely as if they were entering into
a co-partnership in business.
4. From and after the passing of this Act the wages or
earnings during marriage of every husband and the wages
or earnings during marriage of every wife, subject always
to existing contracts of marriage relating thereto, shall
form a common fund appropriated to the maintenance of
their household and family expenses, and the education
and outfit in life of the children of their marriage, in such
way and manner as the parents may see proper. And in
like manner when only the husband is in receipt of
wages or earnings during marriage that income shall
always form a common fund appropriated as aforesaid.
In either case the wife shall be entitled, if she sees proper
to do so, to require one-half of such wages or earnings to
be paid to herself, or to her credit in a bank, and the
same shall be regarded in law as her own separate wages
or earnings, just as if she were in receipt of the same as
wages or earnings, to be applied, however, equally with
her husband’s share, towards the support of the house-
hold.
Apparently, therefore, the wife is to have a
half-share in the old man’s profits, while he
stands all the losses himself. If he makes
£2OOO in his business one year she gets £IOOO.
If he loses £I2OO next year there is nothing
said about her giving back £6OO, so he stands
the whole racket. Therefore, at the end
of two years she has got £IOOO and
he has £2OO less than nothing. Also, it
seems that all his earnings are to go into a fund
for maintaining the family; there is to be
nothing left to form a reserve-fund in his busi-
ness to prevent it smashing up in some future
period of. disaster. The money is to be set
aside for household expenses and the education,
&c., of the children of their marriage. If one
of them has children by a previous marriage, or
lias a mother or grandmother or affiliation-order,
these latter responsibilities are wholly unpro-
vided for. The old law may compel him to
provide for them, and the new law, if it is ever
passed, sets forth that his cash is to go towards
supporting the household and the present batch
of children only. Altogether, there will be
pleasant times ahead for the old man and his
wife, the new woman, if the scheme of the
Maorilaiul Hen Convention ever gets into work-
ing order, and the two will be like Reid and
M‘Millan slanging each other on the floor of
the House over the budget.
A New Precedent.
Q. Full Court seems to have established a new
criminal precedent. Two men, lately convicted
at Rockhampton, were sentenced to imprison-
ment and floggings, under a section of an Act
that provided no flogging penalty. On appeal,
the Full Court cancelled the floggings, but up-
held the imprisonment. It would be interesting
to know how the British Privy Council (in the
always deplorable absence of an Australian
Final Court of Appeal) would deal with an
appeal from the Full Court’s ruling. Hitherto
the legal system has been that a sentence stands
or falls as a whole, and, like the constitutional
money Bill in an “Upper House,” must either
be rejected or endorsed as a whole, but cannot
be amended. However, this is not the first
time that Q. sentences have been varied, though
it is (so far as the writer knows) the first time
that a Q. Judge’s sentence has been publicly
varied by the Full Court. The celebrated
“Hopeful” prisoners, whose death-sentences
were allegedly commuted by the Executive
to penal servitude for life, with a period “in
irons ” that exceeded the legal sentence
“in irons” possible under the Act, had
those commuted sentences varied quietly
by administrative action. A technical ap-
peal at that time would have raised some
curious points. The present Chief Justice of
Q. was then Prime Minister, and chiefly re-
sponsible for the alleged error. In the light of
ordinary horse-sense, there seems no good
reason why an erroneously inflicted sentence
should not be varied by a Full Court, but the
admission of such desirableness should also
necessarily include the possible variation by
any Full Court of a vindictive sentence —vin-
dictive in the sense of undue severity due to
the state of the sentencing Judge’s mind, or
liver, or bias, or anything to which human in-
firmity is subject. In short, there should be
the same right of appeal in criminal as in civil
cases. Much consideration of these and cog-
nate points generally evolves the conclusion
that the trying jury should not only say whether
an accused is guilty, but how much, in its
opinion, he is guilty.
Ed. Bulletin, —The Freetrade Heaven of Un-
limited Cheapness has fallen on Sydney. A
meal of three courses, three pence ; bed and
breakfast, sixpence; pair of shoes, one-and-
nine ; boots, two-and-six ; trousers, three-and-
three ; full suit, ten-and-nine ; wages for coach-
men and gardeners—when they can get them—
five shillings a week and found ; and an im-
mortal soul for eighteen-pence. What more
can be asked ? Between the storekeepers it is
war to the knife, and the tragedy is none the
less real if the ruined homes of the defeated
are known only to the few. Twenty per cent. —
thirty per cent. —discount is placarded on every
other window ; they serve you butter where
you buy bonnets, candles where they sell spades,
and no man is sure when he goes to buy a shirt
that he will not fall over a box of eggs. The
big firms have crushed, or are crushing
out, the smaller ones, and are now fighting
to a finish amongst themselves. Stand at
the railway - station between eight and nine
in the morning, and notice the crowd that
surges into the city. Ninety per cent, are girls
and boys who are doing their parents’ work at
a quarter the money. The Sydney schools
have emptied themselves into the stores and
warehouses. Where, one would like to know,
are the once familiar thousands of able-bodied
men? The lad at 10s. a week is in brisk
demand in town —he is nearly equal to the man
who once got three pounds, if you know how
to boss him. Sydney folk never were very
genial—there is an aloofness in the city’s social
tone reminiscent of the Gallows Hill days—but
now, every man of business is as hard as a steel
wedge and callous as Nosey Bob. He is
otherwise at his peril; for he hears stories
enough of misfortune in one week to strip
him of a month’s profit if he gave a sign of
innate benevolence. But what matters the
souring of the better part of our
natures, the blight of financial worry that
vanishes the smile from every face ? Have
we not Cheapness, that mighty god whose pants
are made at fourpence-halfpenny and shirt at
five farthings ! Can we not dress in shoddy,
and—for a few days—look respectable ? Is
this cheapness genuine ? Is this rubbish with
its fifty per cent, off for cash as good ultimate
value as an article that might cost, say, three
times the price ? Is the ruin of homes, the
breaking of hearts, no item in the bill of cost;
or are we mere counters for money-sharks to
gamble with, stepping-stones in the evolution
of a race of self-degraded serfs ?—F.
Now that the Seddon Government (Maoriland)
has resolved to float a loan of £1,000,000 —
mostly for works which yield no direct revenue
—its principal organ in the South Island —the
Canterbury Times —takes up the parable and
says that the loan is, on the whole, a good and
noble idea. The same paper supported the
non-borrowing policy in former days when its
particular Government believed in financial in-
dependence ; party politics and party news-
papers are built that way. The question now
remains : Is Seddon to be allowed to go on the
loose as Vogel did, or is he to borrow only
under certain definite restrictions ? If borrow-
ing is an evil that can’t be got rid of it
should be restricted in two ways. One
expedient is to enact by statute that no new
loans shall be floated except for works —such as
railways, telegraphs, telephones, the acquire-
ment of estates for sub-division and the like —
which yield direct revenue. The other is to
enact that borrowing must bear such a propor-
tion to the increase of population that the
interest burden per inhabitant shall be a
steadily decreasing one. As an example : The
interest bill for each Maorilander in ’93 was
£2 7s. sd. per annum. If it had then been
enacted that future borrowings must be calcu-
lated to allow of a reduction of this burden at
the rate of at least 4d. per annum, then the
limit in 1896 would be £2 6s. sd. per inhabit-
ant. If, by increase of population or reduction
of interest, the burden had been diminished to,
say, £2 3s. 5d., then there would be room to
borrow another <£3,000,000 at per cent,
without going over the prescribed limit, if Par-
liament felt so inclined. If there was no such
margin, then there could be no borrowing until
there was one. These two restrictions working
together should do at least a little to alleviate
the borrowing disease.
It is alleged in a number of Victorian papers
that the Outtrim Coal Co. runs a kind of truck-
system of its own which is several times more
accursed than the ordinary variety. The town-
ship of Outtrim, it is said, belongs to a syndi-
cate which is half-brother or something to the
coal co., and no miner gets employment from
the coal co. unless he buys an allotment from
the syndicate to build his house on, and pays
for it at an exorbitant rate. Therefore, the
syndicate is doing good business, and land-
values are going up beautifully. It is further
alleged that David Syme, of the Age, is one of
the syndicators; and, under these circum-
stances the owner of the great Liberal
paper would do well to rise up and
clear his character from this accusa-
tion. If the stories of the Outtrim land
arrangements are true (The Bulletin does not
allege that they are, and hopes to see them
promptly disproved), then a time will pos-
sibly come w’hen it will be found necessary to
sack the miners who have finished paying for
their allotments, in order to make room for new
men who will buy more allotments, and thus
keep the business booming. Or a good trade
could be done by buying back the sacked
miner’s allotment at half cost price, and then
selling it at the old figure to the next man, and
so on ad infinitum. There is hardly any end to
the possibilities of a land co. if it can compel
all the working population to buy its land at
its own figure. If even half the allegations
now floating about are correct, Outtrim badly
wants Parliamentary investigation.
New South Wales Justice-Minister Gould
can’t be more than “ almost persuaded ” of the
desirability of appointing shorthand-writers to
courts of justice, and is even timid about try-
ing them in the Equity Court. They are badly
needed in criminal cases, and even in prelim-
inary police-courts when causes are tried that
may involve a committal to a higher court.
The writer knows of a case now before Quarter
Sessions, in which an accused man, who was
committed for trial, on procuring a copy of the
depositions taken by a country C.P.S., dis-
covered that two statements, one by the prose-
cutor himself, and one by a bank-manager,
which would possibly have compelled the
Attorney-General not to “file a bill,” were
omitted altogether. Yet those two statements
were reported in the local newspaper by a con-
scientious reporter quite uninterested in the
case. And shorthand - writers are needed
in court to keep the Judges straight. Any
barrister who has had much to do with appeals
will understand what that means.

The N.S.W. Government is raving once more
over the question of those Govt, stores which
Premier Reid solemnly alleged to cost more than
twice as much in Victoria as in N.S. Wales.
The latest racket comes in the shape of a report
from the N.S.W. Controller-General of Stores.
It admits that the last report was all wrong,
and then it baldly states that the Victorian
stores cost £17,000 instead of £21,000, and that
Freetrade is still on top, but not nearly so much
on top as it seemed to be before. The present
story rests on exactly the same authority as the
other story, which came to grief. Moreover,
there is still no explanation as to why the return
deals with less than one-tenth of the stores
instead of carrying the comparison right through
—in other words, why it is made up by select-
ing only the few items which support the Reid
case, and suppressing those which don’t support
it, or which tell against it. In the present
return, it is admitted that even the N.S. Wales
figures in the last one weren’t right; in fact, the
muddle seems to get worse as it goes on.
Dear Bulletin, — Re underpaid and over-
worked hospital attendants. The inmate attend-
ants in the N.S.W. Govt, asylums, upon whom
all the work falls, are on duty from 4.36 a.m.
to 8 p.m., for which long hours and unhealthy
employment they do not receive any pay.
Further, the so-called rules and regulations,
with the petty, harassing restrictions now
being put in force, have a peculiarly aggravat-
ing effect on people whose perhaps otherwise
kind dispositions are soured and made irritable
by their own infirmities and troubles. Under
these conditions it is not to be expected that
necessary care and attention can be paid to the
more helpless patients, or that reasonable medi-
cal instructions can be rigidly carried out. It
seems as if the system’s sole aim was to make
the inmate feel his unfortunate position as a
pauper in a poor-house.— An Inmate.

SUNDRY SHOWS.
SYDNEY SHOWS FOR COMING WEEK.
Her Majesty’s “A King of Crime.”
Lyceum “La Tosca ”; “ David
Garrick.”
Royalj “Trilby” Burlesque.
Tivoli Rickards’ Variety Co.
MELBOURNE SHOWS FOR COMING WEEK.
Princess’s Nat Goodwin Co.
Royal “ Cheer, Boys, Cheer.”
Opera House Rickards’ Variety Co.
Athenaeum Hall Ovide Musin.
An immense streak of luck, such as even
Bland Holt seldom brought to the old Sydney
Royal, has so far attended Maggie Moore’s
“ Trilby ” burlesque. On an average night every
seat is occupied and the public stands two deep all
round the back of the d. 0., and only extraordinary
gpod fortune will prevent Maggie Moore being
fined (or in default of payment 30 days, and her
hair cut short, and scrub out her own cell) for a
serious case of overcrowding. And the possibility
of Miss Moore scrubbing out her own cell is alto-
gether too painful to contemplate. The reason of
this rush of business is mainly the winsome
Maggie herself, together with Svengaliand Gecko ;
the burlesque, or at least Mr. Finn’s part of it, is
a very small, weak, pale circumstance. All that
that gifted writer has written could be got into
about four sheets of note-paper, or very little
more, unless the scribe wrote laboriously in a very
large hand with his tongue out ; in fact, it is
doubtful if any play ever got through with
less solid literature to back it up. There
is an awful feeling of scantiness about it ;
the skirt of Mr. Finn’s piece ends somewhere
above the knee, and there is no bodice to it, and
when the co. tries to make it go round it suggests
twelve people in one bed with a handkerchief over
them. Also there is no particular brilliance to
make up for this brevity; the puns are common
and moth-eaten, and the humor is as plain in
many places as the countenance of the hardened
female who runs a hash-turret. And this inex-
pensive play has been put on the stage in a pain-
fully cheap fashion —in fact, nothing that looked
much cheaper has been seen at the Royal within
the present writer’s recollection.
* * * *
The co. runs the burlesque on its own with
very little assistance from the libretto, and none
worth speaking of from anybody else. Maggie
Moore plays Trilby with a joyful vim and a wild
desire to show her “fut” to everybody in and out
of season, and she sings various songs which bring
down the house and cause quite a rush of vege-
tables over the footlights. Roberts’ Svengali is a
very fine imitation of Fax’s dirty mesmerist in
his voice and his grime and the clawing of his
long, loamy talons ; and his jungle of unkempt
hair is Fax’s hair to a nicety. Roberts fills in
more gaps in the narrative than anything else, and
if it wasn’t for his business, which is mostly
admirable, the show would end about 9 o’clock.
His rush through the piano, however, is largely
discounted by the fact that no special effort has
been made to make that instrument look real—in
fact, it is a cheap and glaring fake of paper, and
not even brown paper at that—but the versatile
artist does all he can under the circumstances.
Gecko, the third support of the play, is also a
duplicate of Fax’s familiar, and Walter Rivers puts
a lot of valuable comedy into the part, though the
part itself wouldn’t be overcrowded on an envelope.
Rivers looks wildly unutterable, and his hair
makes up for many things. Also, Robert Kennedy
is a much more realistic Laird than the original,
and when he sings his Scotch songs an odor of
haggis and kilt is wafted across the footlights.
The rest of the co. have hardly a remark among
them. “ Trilby,” taking it for all it is worth, is
painfully small, and it tapers off rather feebly at
the end, and the second act (there are only two)
is mainly very ordinary variety show, and all the
efforts of the co. can’t wholly disguise the fact that
there isn’t enough skeleton to hang the meat upon.
Yet it is a huge, roaring success, and the rush of
the multitude on the stairs is a fact against which
there is no arguing.

Sydney Lyceum resumes business on Satur-
day, when Mrs. Potter and Curly Bellew start
three nights of “La Tosca.” Wednesday, Thurs-
day, and Friday of next week are devoted to
“David Garrick,” and on the ensuing Saturday
the co. puts up “ As You Like It,” by the immor-
tal Shakesbacon. The prospect of seeing the fair
Cora in the biking costume of Rosalind has
already sent a thrill through the blase citv of
Sydney.
** # *
Her Majesty’s, Sydney, has been renovated
inside and the electric-light fixings put in order,
so that there is no longer risk (as there lias been
any time in the last year or two) of the show being
suddenly buried in gloom and the audience left to
find its way out by match. Charles Holloway
opens at H.M.’s on Saturday with a new com-
pany, the first production being “ A King of
Crime.” This is the drama which, with Albert
Marsh as the criminal monarch, boomed the same
theatre to a big success in ’94, at a time when it
had long been down among the dust and potsherds ;
but a lot of the show on that occasion consisted of
Marsh, and whether the same luck will attend it
without him is an open question. “A King of
Crime” happens, like most other things, by ar-
rangement with Plain Bill Holloway. This an-
nouncement is so familiar nowadays that the
present writer fully expects some day to fall
downstairs by arrangement with Plain Bill Hol-
loway, and break his neck. Meanwhile, the new
lessee starts with a streak of bad luck, inasmuch
as he couldn’t get the theatre open last Saturday
and make a boom during the temporary eclipse of
the Lyceum. The Bulletin had an idea of deco-
rating this page with a picture, by “Hop,” show-
ing C. Holloway breaking a few things to relieve
his feelings on this painful subject.
** * *
The Tivoli programme is practically the
same as it was last week, the truly marvellous
Frantz Family being still on deck and Mons.
Provo at the bat, as formerly.
** * *
Rev. Charles Clark will last until Satur-
day, when he delivers his final lecture the 130th
which he has given in Sydney. Then he starts
Melbournewards, en route to England, where he
will probably catch a bad cold from the sudden
change of climate and die young, leaving Smythe
an orphan. This is the sort of luck which gene-
rally befalls a man whom the world can’t very well
spare.
*- * * *
The Nerveless Man, one of the most un-
comfortable-looking spectacles that ever went into
the show business, reports that he is about to tour
Queensland, starting at Brisbane. This person’s
speciality is that he seems practically incapable of
feeling pain, and he earns his gruesome oats by
having long pins publicly stuck through him, and
other unpleasantnesses. But for the disfigure-
ment he wouldn’t even mind having a bonfire lit
on his stomach, and in a dull, unemotional sort of
way he really doesn’t care what happens to him.
Outwardly he is an ordinary sort of person with,
seemingly, no constitution to speak of, and there
is a world-weary, shambling aspect about him
which suggests that life has lost its savour. A
man who is practically proof against injury can’t
get the same joy out of dodging cabs that another
man does, and probably this is the reason why the
Nerveless Man looks so tired, and has such a very
aged and haggard soul looking out of two eyes
that are no color in particular.

N.S.W.’s God-fearing Ministry is still
going strong. In the Subukban Times (Sydney)
is printed a programme of a bun-worry in connec-
tion with the City Mission, said bun-struggle
being held in an obscure hall out in the lost-dog
regions of Alexandria, Premier Reid in the chair.
The programme includes “opening hymn,”
“prayer by Rev. J. Green,” “Missionary’s Re-
port,” “address by the Hon. G. Reid,” “solo,
‘ Caller Herrin” (in character),’ a collection, and
an intimation that tickets are Is. each. The good
old God-fearing Ministry ! Consider the chap
that was going to save the country snuffling at a
suburban tea-fight where the prayer of the Rev. J.
Green is mixed up with “Caller Herrin’(incharac-
ter) ’’ —said character consisting mainly of short
petticoats and ankles !
** * *
“The Rivals,” produced at Melb. Prin-
cess’, is Sheridan’s famous comedy altered to suit
the times and satisfy Joe Jefferson, whose part of
Bob Acres has been taken over by Nat Goodwin.
Great masses of the original story are absent from
the American version, and small bits of funny
business figure as modern improvements. The
omitted portions had grown to be considered tire-
some, the “new” items are popular stage tricks
often practised in comic opera, so “ The Rivals,”
in its present form, is better from a managerial
point of view than the play which a bottle-nosed
genius devised for a bygone generation. It is
divided into three acts only, instead of five, and
the curtain falls at about half-past 10.
* * * *
Mr. Goodwin and company give a perform-
ance free from any suggestion of last century. If
the late J. B. Buckstone, Henry Howe, the Chip-
pendales, and others were fairly truthful exponents
of the characters that Sheridan drew, the show
under notice is merely a passable absurdity. The
artists have no manners worth mentioning, nor
are they dressed as, say, the B. and B. company
would dress their parts. In some cases, they hide
their native accent at the expense of naturalness,
Miss Mortimer, as the immortal Mrs. Malaprop,
being the most striking instance in point. She
spouts her droll remarks in a portentous mono-
tone, and probably gets credit for acting her part
well from listeners who haven’t heard it spoken
with more spirit, inflection, and spontaneousness.
Sir Anthony Absolute roars mechanically, but the
humor of his character is not altogether ruined by
the actor’s rough treatment. Sir Lucius O’Trigger
is a tame fire-eater, and the dashing Captain Abso-
lute appears to be an uncomfortable young city clerk
in a military uniform. Bob Acres is converted
into Nat Goodwin. The stupid English yeoman
talks like an American, and fools in the latest
farcical-comedy style. He is not to be thought of
as a real person, but he excites a good deal of
genuine laughter in addition to a large quantity
of forced merriment. Nevertheless Nat Goodwin,
regarded as himself alone, will not enlarge his
Australian reputation with this effort. “ The
Rivals ” is to run for six nights, or even longer.
It may prove the success of the season, thanks to
the author, whose clever creations have not been
put upon the Melb. stage for fifteen long years.

Alice Leamar, John Coleman, La Belle
Maie, and other people who had been away for
some time, came back to Melb. Opera House in a
glittering battalion last Saturday night. They
will glitter for one consecutive week, and no
longer. In fact, they only got there through un-
expected circumstances owing to a delay in the
arrival of Carl Hertz, who makes his first appear-
ance this Saturday.
*# * *
The arrival of Ovide Musin was also de-
layed, but it is now distinctly understood that the
brilliant fiddler will appear at the Athemeum Hall
next Saturday (15th). Madame Trebelli gives a
matinee concert at the Town Hall to-day (Thurs-
day), and a farewell evensong on Saturday. Mean-
while the Bijou, which was to have re-opened last
week, is just as much closed as usual, but the
annual dog-show and conversazione of high-bred
fowls is raging at the Exhibition Building.
“ Cheer Boys Cheer” is not doing so well
as it might at Melbourne Royal, owing to the
smartness of the dialogue, or because a number of
intelligent remarks are put into the mouths of
supernumerary characters who can’t speak them
properly, or because young man Patten is a fatal
lover, br on account of all these things, together
with other causes that attract less attention.
Cheer Boys Cheer ” ought not to fail at the
house where so many feebler and less expensive
entertainments have flourished. The first
act is a marvel of audacious ingenuity in
plot construction, and no gold-mine pro-
moter should forget to see it. Also, the
three following acts are lively, and the
gallantry of George Rignold’s army deserves great
praise, and the last act, although not very excit-
mg, is unusual. The play, on the whole, is a
worthy attempt to get away from musty tradi-
tions. If it doesn’t grow upon the public the
public must bear most of the blame, seeing that
the show has been intelligently cut and condensed
since the opening night, and that the performance
ru is much improved. “Cheer Boys
Cheer ’’ is the first spectacular drama which has
catered for the fat stockbroker who goes to the
theatre for a good larf.

anxious to testify. After a second visit, it strikes me as
one of the pleasantest little plays we have had on the
boards for a long time. It lacks the literary brilliance
and dramatic strategy of Pinero’s and Oscar Wilde’s
smartest, but, as a spell of real life lifted right out of Pike
County and set down before an Australian audience, it is
most convincing. I don’t know Pike County nor Pike
County people personally, but, in ‘ln Mizzoura,’we see
the creations of A. B. Frost’s marvellous pencil in flesh
a <>Ti, u° d ’ and c P ob toe Ravor of quaint naturalness out
of the best material of the American artists in small fiction.
Ihe taciturnity, deliberation, and happy knack of phrase
displayed by Goodwin’s backwoods sheriff are true charac-
tenstics of a peculiarly American growth, if Bret Harte,
Ambrose Bierce, and all the rest of them have not con-
spired to he us into this belief. The sheriff’s pretty sister,
the slatternly girl who is always tired, the long, lean
coachbuUder s assistant, whose limbs poke suddenly out
of his clothes, and who, although working all day and half
the night, continually laments his want of a ‘ stiddv job,’
ana the practical, idiomatic housewife, all ring true ;>s
teal American raw products, and Goodwin’s team are very
™ ucb at home here. I hope the management will start
the Sydney season with ‘ In Mizzoura.’ ”
Musgrove has measured Nellie Stewart for a theatre
and is building it in London. This is what comes of
having a beautiful actress on hand ; you naturally have to
build a theatre round her. Then if she goes away you
spend the rest of your life hunting for someone who can
wear the building’, and it remains a burden on your soul
for ever afterwards.
