Emeiie Melville attended last week’s meeting of Melb.
Shakespeare (society, at Dr. Neild’s invitation, and read
some passages from “ King Lear.” A subsequent discus-
sion as to King Lears age gave an opening fora happy
leinark by schoolmaster Wilson. Age, said Mr. W., was a
variable thing as in the case of Dr. Neild, who was gene-
ia y regarded as quite elderly. But, after noting
him in conversation with Miss Melville, one felt convinced
that the president of the society was a very young man.
Whereat the Shakespearians got as near to roaring with
laughter as their intellectual culture would allow them.
The musical enthusiasts of Coolgardie and Perth mean
to do their little bit in the way of sending an Australian
voice Ome to be trained. Miss Neva Carr-Glynn, a short,
thick-set young lady, with a rather taking but, as yet,
rough, contralto, and not the very best ear in the world
(judging from her ballad warbling with the Rickards
companies), is the Westralian conception of “ another
Ada Crossle}'.” Miss Neva, by the way, is Mrs. Walter
Baker s sister. She makes the 59th “coming Orossley,”
of whom The Bulletin has been advised within the last
year. Why pick and choose between all these candidates
for culture at the public expense? Better to raise one
vast subscription and confide every woman in Australia
between the ages of 17 and 60 to the tuition of Madame
Marchesi, in Paris. No doubt Markasy would reduce her
terms for a wholesale order.
In the Trebelli concert advts. Maggie Stirling is termed
a contralto. Miss Stirling, who has commenced to display
her beautiful voice with real artistic taste in some num-
bers, is an undeniable mezzo.
Herr Kretschmann, one of Sydney’s most cultured
musicians, has publicly brought forward another of his
clever pupils. Miss Yvonne Leverrier, a pianiste, recently
appeared with the Marshall Hall orchestra in Melbourne
so successfully that she was immediately captured by the
Metropolitan Liedertafel to play at its concert. Later,
Herr Kretschmann will parade his pupil before Sydnej
audiences. Another of the veteran German’s pupils who
created a furore with her playing in Sydney is the gifted
Miss Esther Kahn.
The American mummers now in Melbourne comment
on the dullness of that city at night, also on its general
inhospitality. The latter, however, is due not to intention
but to stupidity. Nobody in Melbourne knows how to
approach and entertain a stranger. “ Australians when
they come to America are rushed,” said a leading member
of the co., “ but Americans here are just allowed to pass
through.”
At Melb. Princess on the first night of “Mizzoura”
manager Tallis had to ask a dress-circle johnny to stop
laughing. The laugh was like an exaggerated sea-gull’s
“ craik, craik,” and was spoiling every pathetic passage
of the play.
“ Pat” Bathurst has succeeded to Brovvnlow’s parts in
the Williamson and Musgrove Opera Co., and according
to Maoriland press notices has made a success of them.
This clever young mummer is better known to the world
of Sydney and Bathurst as Pat Kenna, formerly a member
of Sydney Liedertafel, and at one time U.S. Vice-Consul.
After a career as an amateur baritone he did two years
“ poverty-pointing ” in India and the East, playing every-
thing from light opera to tragedy, and from pitch-and-
toss to high-treason. Upon his return to Australia he
appeared in the ill-fated “Morocco Bound,” and after-
wards joined the Firm, making his first hit in “ Miss
Decima.” His legs are not Ryley’s, and his voice isn’t
Browniow’s ; but he can act better than one and sing
better than the other, and should have a decent show in
his native country.
Henry Lawson’s book of prose yarns will
be ready next week (post free, ss. 6d.), and will
be, from many points of view, one of the most fasci-
nating volumes issued in Australia. Frank
Mahony contributes eight; fine illustrations.
Orders sent in to this office will be posted
promptly on issue.
Louise Mack’s serial story reaches a crisis
in August Cosmos, and makes it clearer than ever
that monthly serials should be suppressed by
literary law. “Esotericus” pessimises over Aus-
tralia’s future; and Editor Montgomery inner-
views Bland Holt and wife with illustrations and
agreeable results. Geo. Essex Evans rhymes
neatly and numerously, “Titus Salt ” humouresces
characteristically concerning a unique voyage
through Magellan’s Straits ; and there is a contri-
bution on aboriginal art by W. A. Squire, and one
by W. Harding on a man-eating plant which was
poisoned by a packet of arsenic in the pocket of
its all-but victim. Agnes Macready gives some
pretty and poetic verses, though the close seems
rather feeble and conventional ; and N. V. rhii-
pott gets some stirring emotion into a story which
hinges on the not-new situation of a woman being
saved from flight with hubby’s friend by the
“new life stirring”—as if the “new life wasnt
obvious previously. Altogether the number is
well-varied, and the quality is improving.

“ I have been 30 years at the Bar and on
the Bench, and I think that, when a man has
served such a period in the profession, he ought
to think of making way for a younger man ” —
Judge Williams to an Age interviewer. His
Honor asserts he gave up a practice of .£6000
a-year to accept the Judgeship, and complains
that he has done two men’s work ever since. But
it makes The Bulletin snort to hear Judges talk
of the money-sacrifice involved in going from Bar
to Bench. When a barrister takes a Supreme
Court Judgeship, he knows that he becomes
entitled to a life-pension of .£1700 or ,£1800 a-year
if he becomes partially paralysed five minutes
after being gazetted.

The Wild-Cat Column .
Premier Reid somehow can’t even present a
bald statement of figures in the Gazette without mis-
representing the facts to boom his own system of finance.
The N.S.W. revenue for July, ’96, was £107,209 less than
that, for July, ’95, and a footnote is appended, pointing
out that £88,829 of this decline is caused by the abolition
of certain Customs duties, so “ the decrease for the month
on the basis of the present tariff ”is only £18,380. The
real state of things is that G. Beid lost during the month
£88,829 by taking off Customs duties, and gained £22,733
by putting on the income-tax, therefore the real decline
in the revenue—that is to say, the decline which tends to
show how much worse things are this year than in July,
’95, is not £18,380 but £41,112. The utter impossibility
of getting G. Beid to tell the whole truth about anything
is a strange and crab-like circumstance.
* » * *
The new financial year starts badly in two pro-
vinces. The July revenue in Victoria is £3364 less than
the revenue for the corresponding month last year. And
in N.S.W. the July revenue is £107,209 less than that for
July, ’95.
J » * * *
The Seddon Govt, is going to float a £1,000,000
loan on the ground that it is impossible to carry on public
works without raising more money. Why is it ? Between
1889 and 1895 the annual interest on the public debt was
reduced by conversion operations and otherwise from
£1,866,000 to £1,656, 000-saving £210,000 per annum.
Also, the population increased so much, ancl there are
now so many more people to bear the liability that the
reduction in the burden is really much greater even than
it looks. Maoriland could put up nearly £500,000 a year
in extra taxation for public works —in which case there
would be no need for a loan—and yet not be burdened
any more heavily than it was in 1890. And as it got
through in 1890 and in other years that were even worse,
there is every reason to believe that it could do so now —
if the Ministry wasn’t hankering after votes for next
general election.
& * * * *
They all do it more or less. For the coming
year the Victorian Govt, proposes spending £286,000 of
borrowed money on roads, drainage, and other works that
yield no direct revenue —the money to be repaid by in-
stalments in about 15 years if nothing goes wrong. Pro-
bably something will go wrong. Queensland proposes
spending about £160,000 of borrowed funds on big guns,
ammunition, dredging, and other non-revenue-producing
jobs which properly belong to current expenditure.
Maoriland is going to spend £500,000 of borrowed cash on
road-making and similar works of ordinary expenditure ;
and N.S. YVales is apparently going to “blew” about
£600,000 of loans in the same fashion/ The great art and
mystery of surplus-making. *
The usually patient Brisbane Courier has risen
m revolt, and demands a strict investigation into the
affairs of the Queensland National Bank before it is
permitted to further reconstruct the £3,000,000 or more
of Government money which it still holds. It describes
the Q.N.B. as the most reticent banking institution in
Australia, and the accusation is very nearly correct.
Among the reconstructs of 1893, the City of Melbourne
frankly burst up. The North Queensland and Commer-
cial of Sydney released their reconstructed deposits in
full. The E.S. and A. reconstructed again after a rigid
overhaul of its accounts. The National, Victoria, London,
A.J.S., and Boyal of Queensland are paying off their
liabilities by instalments. Among the banks which asked
for further assistance from their creditors, the Q.N.B.
is the only one which kept on paying dividends to
its ordinary shareholders even when it was known to
almost every one that it would have to get more
concessions from depositors. Its balance-sheet doesn’t
even profess to show, like those of the A.J.S. and
Commercial of Australia, how the liquidation, of the old
boom assets is getting on. No one knows by its balance-
sheet whether it is doing any new business at all ;
whether it has any new deposits or only those which are
so fixed that they can’t get away ; whether any of the 4J
per cent, deposits have ever been paid off ; how much the
locked-up assets from the pre-smash days amount to;
whether any progress has been made at liquidating them ;
whether there is' no loss by depreciation or whether the
loss amounts to a million or several millions ; or anything
else worth speaking about. The lumping together of
reconstructed Go vernment deposits, new ditto, reconstruc-
ted private deposits, new private deposits, current
accounts and “other liabilities” makes it impossible to
tell how public confidence stands, or falls down, in the
Q.N.8., and the lumping together of the old assets,
whose unrealisability brought about the 1893 sus-
pension, with the new ones, shuts out any informa-
tion as to whether the former are being realised, and, if
so, how fast, and whether they are being loaded up
with unrealisable interest or not—in fact, the whole
s tuation is a mystery. Public opinion, as evinced by the
share-market, values Q.N.B. deposits lower than those of
the Commercial of Australia (which showed i 24,000 loss
last half-year as against the Q.N.B’s alleged £lO,OOO
profit), and its shares (£5 10s. paid-up) are constantly
quoted “no buyers.” The Q.N. Bank, in fact, is the
financial conundrum of Australia, and it wants an inde-
pendent overhaul very badly—especially considering that
£3,000,000 or more of public money is at stake.
* * * •
It is solemnly reported that the Maoriland
Government won’t ask Parliament this session to “ raise
money ” for starting a State Fire Insurance Office. How
much money does such an office want “ raised? If the
State will advance £lO,OOO from the trust-funds for pre-
liminary expenses (to be repaid out of the first year’s or
two or three years’ premiums), and deposit with the office
£250,000 of State 31 per cent, debentures which it can sell
to raise money in the unlikely event of a big conflagration
and a very heavy bill for losses striking it before the pre-
miums have accumulated sufficiently (the debentures to
bear interest only when they pass out o, the department’s
hands), the institution would be afloat. The chief argu-
ment now advanced against the proposal is that it would
oust all other fire offices from Maoriland, therefore there
would be no other offices to re-insure big risks with, and
“ the largest fire insurance office in the world couldn’t
venture to insure whole cities on its own sole responsi-
bility.” Which is true as far as existing offices are con-
cerned. But then the biggest existing fire office has
about £6,000,000 of capital and reserves, and the Maori-
land State Office would have a capital of over £200,000,000
—in fact, its capital would be the whole country and
everything in it and all its credit outside. A concern like
that could insure the biggest risk in Maoriland “ on its
own,” and not feel it.
* * * *
The Sydney and Suburban Hydraulic Power
Co. reports that the half-year’s net profits are £3147, and
a dividend at the rate of 7J per cent, per annum absorbs
£2812, leaving the ro. with a total profit and loss balance
of £2210. The Hydraulic Co’s assets include a claim on
a guarantee co., now awaiting appeal, of £939, and 1600
law-costs, both of which items may be recovered if the
case is decided in the Hydraulic’s favor, while if it goes
wrong these and the further law-costs will have to be
written off, which will mop up most of the above balance.
Another asset is “Parliamentary rights and concessions”
(i.e., goodwill), £15,000, and a third is “preliminary ex-
penses.” These items are eminently shadowy, and at the
date of last balance-sheet the Hydraulic Co. had really
only £98,880 of visible assets against over £109,000 of
solid-looking liabilities to shareholders, debenture-holders,
and other creditors. A concern in this condition would
do well to pay a smaller dividend and devote the balance
to writing down the alleged goodwill.
* » * *
The balance-sheet of the Bank of North Queens-
land, which was commented upon last week, appears in
another part of this issue. The profits, though small,
show a remarkable improvement on last half-year’s
figures, and the solidity of the institution, so far as con-
cerns depositors and other creditors, is conspicuous.
* * * *
Balmain New Ferry Co. reports that the net
profits for the half-year ended 30th June are £1670, which
is apparently a slight falling-off from last year’s rate by
reason of increased expenses. An interim dividend at the
rate of 10 per cent, per annum absorbs £672, and, after
writing off £641 for depreciation and towards preliminary
expenses, the co. is still £357 to the good. The previous
half-year’s balance of £663 makes a total of £lO2O to credit
of profit and loss. By an extraordinary process of book-
keeping, however, the £5OO set aside last half-year as a
reserve for maintenance of steamers has been included
among the receipts, making the profit and loss balance
£1520. It is further explained that the directors have
applied this reserve towards building a new vessel.
Another item, “ Reserve Fund, £304,” which ap-
peared in last balance-sheet, has also, apparently,
made an unaccountable disappearance, and so has an
item described as “ Accrued from forfeited shares,
£276.” As far as the visible evidence goes, it would
seem that the directors of the co. cheerfully apply the re-
serves to purposes they were not specifically intended for
very much at their own discretion. The co. is decidedly
prosperous, however, and, except for the amazing manner
in which its accounts are presented, and the way its re-
serve funds come and go without any visible explana-
tion, there isn’t much to complain of. Still, this latter
point ought to be cleared up. There are plenty of com-
panies where the reserves vanish from among the assets,
but a concern where they blow right off the balance-sheet
is an anomaly.
*' * * *
The Commercial Building and Investment Co.
(Sydney), one of the few survivors of the great breakage
of a year or two ago, reports that the year’s income from
rents was only £10,267 —equal to about 4 per cent, on
nominal values; and as the expenses were £13,794 the
concern went £3527 to the bad on the year’s work. It is
further added that during the last 12 months things have
got worse on the whole than they were before, and rents
have dropped, and the demand for properties has dimin-
ished, and nothing cheering has happened.
* * * *
The Civil Service Mutual B. and I. Society
(Sydney), which had the luck to be formed after the boom
had collapsed, so that it didn’t get loaded up with bricks
and mortar at impossible prices, finished its fourth year
on 30th June. The net profit is £453, as against £435 for
previous year. A div. at 5 per cent, per annum absorbs
£379, and the balance (£74) is added to profit-and-loss,
making it £928. Of this, £5OO is employed to start a
reserve-fund, and £428 is carried forward. The society is
a prosperous-looking affair on a small scale —the capital
is onlv £15,250, and deposits £2291. Also, it is quite un-
intentionally a powerful essay on the necessity for a State
Land Bank. Its chief asset is “ Loans, £12,149,” and as
the interest for the year is £1302 it would appear that the
miserable borrower pays 10 per cent, for his accommoda-
tion. Working-expenses and depreciation of the office-
furniture absorb over £7OO, so this small concern has
actually to charge its borrowers about 5)4 per cent, to
cover working-expenses alone, and another 4J4 per cent,
to earn a very moderate return for its shareholders.
Against this, the Maoriland State Loan Department works
at an expense of about 15s. per cent., and the difference
is all saved to the borrower. Said difference lies in the
fact that one institution keeps an office, six directors, two
auditors, a manager, a surveyor, and a firm of solicitors
to look after less than £20,000, and the other keeps about
the same amount of pomp to look after £1,500,000. The
small twopenny building society must be an extortioner
to live ; the enormous ratio of expenses makes moderation
impossible. * * *
The assay fiend is still large and rampant at
Coolgardie. In the absence of regular crushings “ assays ”
are the usual emergency, and but for newspapers they
would have no visible lawful means of support. “ Three
samples of stone from the Perseverance mine have assayed
260 z. gold, 61oz. silver; 370 z. gold, B‘2oz. silver ; 450 z.
gold, 87oz. silver.” Kalgoorlie sends along a 630 z. assay
from “a rich strike” made at Broad Arrow. Any paper
that pays for and publishes such news does not know its
business, and is being played upon by boomers for boom
purposes. How is it we never have any assays from
Charters Towers ?
* * * *
One of the experts who appeared before the Life
Assurance Board of Inquiry (Melbourne) made the startling
assertion that “it would be a most pernicious thing to
attempt to state what was the standard of solvency ; the
standard set up would necessarily have to be a very mild
one.” The news that the standard would have to be a
very mild one —presumably because some of the offices
couldn’t otherwise get through—is interesting to insurers.
And why, in the name of all that is wonderful, would it
be “most pernicious” to define what is solvency, instead
of leaving societies to be as insolvent as they please ? The
actuai’y’s business is an exact science—so exact that the
extent of any society’s liabilities can be ascertained with
almost absolute accuracy. It would be no hardship to
enact that no society must reckon on its assets producing
more than, say, 3J per cent, or 4 per cent, interest. And
beyond that, the standard would simply require that the
office should have enough assets, on an independent
valuation, to cover its liabilities, which are easily ascer-
tained. In what possible way is it “most pernicious” to
require by law that a society’s assets must cover its
liabilities? Such evidence is calculated to set the public
wondering how the witness’s own society, if he is con-
nected with one, would stand investigation.
* * * *
The liquidators of the English and Australian
Mortgage Bank, which is just paying a second div. of 6d.
in three .years, propose to lump its assets with those of
sundry other Daviesian institutions, so that there may be
one vast collection of unsaleable properties under uniform
management, instead of several rival lots, each holding
hopeless auction-sales “on its own.” The father of this
proposal is Mr. Kay, the liquidator who wasn’t allowed to
give unpleasant evidence at the trial of the Mercantile
Bank directors.
* * * *
The Crown Prosecutor’s indictment of the City
of Melbourne Bank’s last balance-sheet gave Alfred
Deakin, for the defence, an opportunity to revive his pet
legal argument—they all did it. “ After declaring a divi-
dend of £20,000 within one little month, ere the ink was
dry on the balance-sheet, their doors were closed,” said
prosecutor Finlayson. “ How many other banks closed
their doors ?” asked Deakin, as though his precious conun-
drum were pertinent to the case. The other day, in the
course of the Mildura inquiry, a witness testified to the
honesty of Deakin as a man and a politician. Deakin the
lawyer, when engaged in bank oases, is an unblushing
preacher of the Smellbourne gospel that ten blacks make
a white.
* * * *
Smellbourne is divided between two ideas re
the City of Melb. Bank prosecution. One, that the trial
is meant to show indisputable cause for the Companies
Act amendments, drafted by Attorney-General Isaacs.
The other, that it is a sop to British creditors, who, up to
now, have had no return for the pile of money spent in
talking about criminal proceedings against other directors.
Anyway, the C. of M. Bank case is open to suspicion.
How on earth could Chairman Loader and colleagues be
found guilty whilst a dozen, or two, of false balance-
sheeters roamed at large ? And if they were found guilty,
how could they be put into the prison from which bank-
manager Staples was secretly set free? Smellbourne’s
morality must be consistent.
* * * *
N.S.W. Chief Secretary Brunker is in a blissful
state of ignorance about the doings of private “labor
registry offices.” He is “ not aware ” that some of these
so-called labor-agents charge employers nothing at all,
nor that they milk the possible salaries of employes far in
advance, before even “ laying them on” to employers.
Mr. Brunker probably “ nevah reads the papahs”—except
their “God-fearing” portions. He is “considering”
whether these Labor vampires should be registered and
supervised by the State. And this in a land that sells
licenses even to milk-vendors. “ Considering ! ”
* * * *
The alliance of theN.S.W. penny-postage move-
ment with the scheme to impose a halfpenny postage-fee
on newspapers (within the province) must inevitably lead
to the abolition of the special class subsidy to the metro-
politan journals. Postmaster-General Cook, under the
Cabinet whisperings of Stage Prompter Reid, opposes
both proposals on the ground that penny-postage will
take away £50,000 worth of revenue, and that, judging by
Victoria’s experience, a halfpenny newspaper postage-
rate will only yield £15,000. Cook says that in Victoria
15,000,000 newspapers are posted annually, and, at £d.
each, only yield £15,000. Cook’s £, therefore, contains
500 pence, inasmuch as 15,000,000 halfpence equal £31,250.
Cook has evidently graduated in arithmetic under “ Eye-
glass ” Reid—the monocular “A B ” being “2 and 2 make
27.”
* • * *
Three months ago kaolin was found at the 500 ft.
level in the Broken Hill Proprietary mine—the first ever
found at that depth. Then the stuff was very poor, but
lately, when struck at another point, the ore shows an
improvement. Welcome, of course, and geologically in-
teresting ; but a good smelting ore, though possessed of
much smaller metallic contents, would be more valuable
to the co.
* # * •
Concerning a recent Bulletin par. about a kaolin
discovery' somewhere or other. New-chum silver-pro-
spectors, hearing much of the Broken Hill kaolin develop-
ments, are apt to conclude, when they strike kaolin, that
they have got into another Broken Hill. But kaolin is
merely fine clay, and its occurrence as a silver gangue is
phenomenal. And, anyway, even at Broken Hill, it is
about the most uncertain of all the deposits.
* ♦ • *
The North and South B.H. Co’s amalgamate to
secure Mr. D. Edwards as engineer, to have full charge of
the mills. Good biz. A miner, even a good miner,
doesn’t necessarily know anything of fine ore-dressing by
patent processes. The South mill, though, doesn’t want
much managing ; the North mill wants a lot of it, partly
because it is ah unholy makeshift mixture of old and new
machinery, but more because of the extreme variableness
of the ore, aforetime commented on.
* * * *
According to chairman M'Bryde, at the half-
yearly meeting of B.H. Proprietary Go., “it would be
impossible to pay a shilling dividend monthly if the
yield was not up to the usual standard, and the proba-
bility was that until the end of the year they would only
be able to pay a div. every second month; but share-
holders need not fear that it would not continue to be a
div.-paying mine.” A Bulletin par. the other week was
to the effect that on last half-year’s figures Is. divs. could
now be paid regularly until January next, when one
would have to be passed. But now the directors have
decided upon treating 5000 tons of sulphides weekly ; the
average last half-year was under 800 tons a week. Which,
of course, makes all the difference. And the decision, too,
means all the difference between discreet and indiscreet
management.
* * * *
British mill running on carbonate —not sul-
phides, as it was going to do in view of the alleged Burn-
ham Syndicate agreement. Apparently that agreement
is “off,” never, perhaps, having been completely “on.”
And some hundreds of poor beggars are left nursing a
very expensive baby acquired on the strength of positive
statements that an agreement had been concluded. The
responsibility for the whole bungle wants fixing some-
where. Still the mine has its prospects, though divs. on
such a capital must on present developments be micro-
scopic.
* * * *
Fitzgerald Moore, one of the best known of
what the daily papers call “ mining men,” died, the other
day, at Coolgardie. Moore, who was one of the most
plausible of people, had a hand in a hundred different
schemes in all parts of the world, and few of them suc-
ceeded. Mostly, though, Mr. Moore knew when to get
out. An engineer by profession, he was the introducer of
both smelting and concentrating methods on the Barrier,
and through his agency a smelter and afterwards a mill
were erected at the Pinnacles, which claim he introduced
to the public 11 years ago. The little affair of the Round
Hill boom seven years back “cruelled” him, until the
Coolgardie gamble set him on his feet again.
The Pinnacles, by the way, seems to be the
Deeming of mining. Moore, who floated the claim
originally ; Lamont, who built and ran the smelting fur-
nace ; G. M‘L. Matheson, who promoted the Cockburn-
Pinnacles-Broken Hill tramway ; Greenway, the broker
and biggest dealer in Pinnacles in the early days—all are
dead, though all were comparatively young men.

one generation in all N.S.W. camps.
* * * *
Wyalong mines—Bolte’s, 7 tons of pyrites, 1330 z.;
Red Flag, 26 tons, 560 z. ; Keep-it-Dark, 127 tons, 201 oz. ;
Golden Fleece, 400 tons, 5700 z.; N.S. Wales, 18 tons, 109 oz.
In the absence of leases, this camp has had more legitimate
mining, perhaps, but very much less boom ; and many
Wyalongites would prefer to have it the other way about.
All the available boom of Australia is monopolised by
Westralia. Anyway, there is not enough boom on this
continent to go right round.
* * * *
A mining case of considerable interest was
decided by the N.S.W. Full Cojurt on 7th inst. It was
sought to quash a conviction made by the Newcastle
P.M., under which appellant, manager of the Newcastle-
Wallsend Co., was fined for not supplying sufficient air to
the men in the colliery. The case depended upon the
meaning of certain words in rule 3 of the Coal Mines
Regulation Act, which states that an adequate amount
of ventilation shall mean not less than 100 cubic feet per
minute for each man, boy, and horse, which shall sweep
undiminished along the airway past each working place.
The point at issue was whether “ working place ” meant
merely the entrance of the bord, or the actual “ face ” at
which the miner was at work. The magistrate had held
that the air-current had to be taken to the men ; the Full
Court, however, was of opinion that it was enough if it
should sweep past the mouth of the bord in which they
were working. It was stated by one witness, and adopted
bv the Chief Justice, that if the current swept past the
entrance the whole bord would be ventilated by diffusion.
Nothing could be further from the truth. The smoke
generated by a shot fired early in the morning at the face
of a bord is often not dissipated in the afternoon, eight
hours later, though a full current is sweeping all day past
the entrance of the bord.

The Sunlight G.M. Hillgrove, N.S.W., is giving
a lesson in economical mining. The royalty has also been
reduced. It should shortly be in a position to pay a
small regular monthly div.

The Bulletin is pleased to state, after making
full inquiry, that the amount of money spent on the sur-
face of the Pambula G.M. Co.’s property is insignificant
compared to the amount expended beneath it, and that
the mine is worked in a miner-like manner.

Stated that Tiger Inglis has secured under offer
the Baker’s Creek Extended, N.S.W. He will place it on
the London market. Present shareholders to receive
£3OOO cash and 30 per cent, in paid-up shares; £20,000
working capital to be furnished. The success of this
show depends entirely on the dip of the Baker’s Creek
run of gold, which seems to be slowly leaving the parent
mine.

G-undagai, N.S.W. : —“ Specimens have been
secured from the New Kimo reef of great richness. Only
a trial crushing of a ton has been treated. About 15 tons
at grass expected to yield Boz. per ton. The reef is not
yet properly defined, and the various geological forma-
tions appear to be jumbled up. The owners are jbreating
for the sale of the property, asking the big price of
£30,000.” Well, ain’t it a free country? Any fellow own-
ing a reef “ with the various geological formations all
jumbled up,” and 15 tons of rich ore -at grass, can ask
£300,000 for it if he likes.

“ Mr. Fearby gives a glowing account of a W.A.
find—Morgan and Millar’s. While taking out samples
with his pick, he unearthed one solid piece of gold, 8m.
long and 2in. thick. It is the most wonderful show he
has ever seen in his life, and surpasses anything yet
seen in W.A.” A hunk of gold this size should turn the
scale at 180oz. Alas ! we have all read too many glowing
accounts of the same sort re W.A. mines which subse-
quently closed down for want of gold. P.S. —Did Fearby
hang liis hat on that hunk ?
* * * *
The prospectus of the Gen. Gordon Junction
gold-mine, which got a premature mention in Tire Bulle-
tin through a proof-sheet going astray, was published
from Adelaide last week. As first stated, the G.G.
Junction is a spec of 80,000 ten shilling shares issued at
five shillings, the vendor taking 30,000 shares and £6000
cash. The mine proposes to start with an available work-
ing capital of £6000 odd, and the reports of G. R. Fearby
and others are very good, as reports go. Portions of the
Gen. Gordon main' lode are said to crop up within the
property, also another lode 100ft. wide appears to pass
through its centre, wherefore the prospectus breathes a
hopeful spirit which may prove to be quite justifiable.
On the authority of Bewick and Moreing, Lon-
don engineers, the bulk of the stone of Bayley’s will go
6dwt., but the occurrence of rich pockets should bring it
up to 12 and 15dwt. Cost of mining and milling is put
down at £1 a ton, and amalgamation with the South and
a mine to the north is recommended. What has become
of the “wonderfully rich patch” struck in the mine a
week or two back ?
* * * *
The report of meeting of shareholders of the
Great Blow mine held in London is a pitiable
instance of J. Bull’s credulity. Captain W. Bell-
M‘Taggart, chairman, said there was a million tons of ore
in sight. He excused a lOdwt. bulk assay thus :—“ When
making a fire assay it was necessary to take out every
speck of gold that can be seen with the strongest micro-
scope.” Although they had this great mass of stone,
“which they could actually touch, sit down, and have
their dinner on, they had no water ; all their money was
gone, and unless some more was at once found, the mine
stood in jeopardy of being jumped.” Actual expenditure—
To purchase of mine, £25,000 cash (and £100,000 in shares);
spent on the mine, £16,490, and in London, £3728; in
machinery, £9720. The gallant Capt. read a long letter from
a “ celebrated mining man,” whose name was not disclosed,
who incidentally mentioned when he came to the value
of the ore as an average—“ I regret my instructions bind
me to leave these points untouched.” This the capt.
“ regarded as an extremely satisfactory letter.” He also
regarded the expenditure as extremely satisfactory “ con-
sidering we have ore to the value of one and a-half
millions in sight.” It was decided to reconstruct the eo.,
and everybody went away pleased with the mine and
their good judgment in going into it. First a Bull, next a
milch Cow, then a Goat.

* * * *
Want of sufficient water is the everlasting curse
of the Coolgardie goldfields, which great and irremove-
able trouble has impressed the Age and Sydney D.T.
special mining commissioner—“ The mines may be divided
into three classes—those that have lodes but no water,
those that have water but no lodes, and those that have
neither lodes nor water.” He truly regards it as the
great black outlook which will be the death of scores of
mines that could be worked to pay moderate profits if
only they were surrounded by favorable conditions. He
adds that as things now go the number of mines that will
pay dwindles down to an insignificant total. “ A resident
of Kanowna” writes in strong protest, naturally, and
refers to the number of instances where water has been
struck at shallow depths—but is careful to say not a word
about how suddenly these supplies disappear. A country
with next to no rainfall, with no rivers, not even creeks,
and no hope of an artesian supply, may have plenty of
wind but insufficient water. The little it does get is so
densely salt that much is lost by condensation. They can
therefore never have cheap mining in W.A.
* * * *
The General Gordon crushing, it seems, is not
so much overdue as behind the time appointed for it by
rumor. The co. was floated in May, in June the battery
was ordered, in July it was despatched westward, and in
September the crushing will begin to happen. Predic-
tions among those interested vary from 5oz. to 7oz. on the
stuff now being raised. And the next crop is entirely in
the hands of Providence.
* * * *
An officer sent from the Mines Department re-
ported well of the gold discovery at Black Flat, about a
dozen miles from Melbourne. Quartz leaders, showing
coarse gold, promise to thicken as they go lower at the
first find, and similar leaders have been unearthed freely
in the neighboring country, A gold-field at Oakleigh
would be a blessed thing for one or two Melbourne
breweries, anyhow.

♦ * * *
“ A Churchman ” has rushed into Sydney
print to point out that, whereas a male missionary
to China (or other barbaric lands) only risks “ his
life,” a female missionary risks “ more than life it-
self—her honor.” Why magnify the fascination
of the danger ?
* * * *
A parson located at Adelaide, writes to
a local paper to state on his own authority that
the Almighty objects to the popular respect paid
to Premier Kingston. “ There is a God in
Heaven,” he says, “and He is justly displeased
with South Australia.” The parson always mis-
takes himself for the Almighty in this way. The
reverend is aggravated at Kingston, therefore
he assumes that God must be also aggravated, and
states, the fact without any reservation whatever.
T his is why it is never any use arguing with a
parson. An ordinary man tries to prove that his
own little fad is. right. A parson merely says
that God regards it as right; and, if you ask
how he learned the fact, he says “ Go forth, thou
blasphemer !” or words to that effect. Then, if
you ask for further particulars, he says that you
will be Lost for evermore.
* * * *
The Melbourne parson who sued an under-
taker the other day for burial-fees seems to have
got the shepherd business down on a plain business
footing. He was entitled, he said, to Bs. for each
burial m the Lpiscopal end of the cemetery,
whether he read the service or not. The under-
taker was supposed to hustle round and collect
the money from the friends of the corpse, and
hand it over. In this case the reverend wanted
<413 12s. for 34 burials ; 90 burials having already
been paid for. The undertaker refused to pay on one
corpse on the ground that it was of the Catholic
faith, which the parson denied; and he further
stated that he had given notice that he would be
dashed, or words to that effect, if he collected fees
for this parson any longer, and therefore his re-
sponsibility was ended. Moreover, he seemed to
imply that the parson was cantankerous and un-
reasonable, and not a nice man to do business
with. The parson averred that the whole trouble
arose because he wouldn’t pay the hearse-man 12J
per cent, commission for collecting, and the person
with the ghostly vehicle naturally wanted to know
why he should act gratis as any parson’s collector.
There was a lot about “ the account ” and the
‘account rendered,” and, in a general way, the
parson said “ Yah ” and the undertaker said
. Booh. This sort of thing throws Quite a new
light on the pathetic burial of Little Eva.

Akaroa, the French
settlement in Maoriland,
possesses, or possessed, an
Enoch Arden. A French-
man, leaving his wife and
family behind him, went
on a trip to Paris. He
entirely disappeared, leav-
ing no trace whatever.
Nine or ten years passed
and still he did not return
or communicate with his
friends ; so the wife, con-
cluding that he was dead,
married a stalwart Irish-
man. Great was the sur-
prise of everyone when at
last the former husband
turned up. It seems that
when he reached France
he was arrested for no .
having served his military
term, and deported to
Algeria. He had been
stationed on the frontier of
that heat-stricken sand-
ocean for ten years, and
not permitted to write to
his friends or hold any
communication with the
outside world. He re-
turned to find he had no
home, nor wife, nor family
and, as liis supplanter ga \,
him to understand througn
mutual friends that any
attempt to restore himself
to the bosom of his family
wouldbe sternly resented,
he went off into the world
somewhere, an outcast.

Q. Govt, has a curious leprosy-notice of
several clauses posted at stations on the Normnn-
ton-Croydon railway. The pith of it is that
leprosy is a harmless and non-infeetious disease,
“ CHASTENED RAPTURE .”
(In an Australian Anglican church, first Sunday after Palestinian lecturer Haskett-Smith.)
THE CURATE (loq.) : “ Imagine, my dear brethren, my chastened rapture whilst gazing at the
picture of a rock on which at apostle might have sat.”
and the public are not to be frightened of sitting
m a rail way-carriage crowded with lepers—in
tact they could eat raw leper daily with no bad
effect (to themselves). The last clause of the
notice states that the Govt, and medical authori-
ties know nothing about leprosy.

(N.b.W.) River held a corroboree at Kempsey
show-grounds recently. The “stage-manage/’
was a native convert, and the chief item on the
programme represented a band of nigs, endeavoring
GEQGRAPHY OF THE BACKBLOCKS.
BILLY THE ONE-EYE (who is hushed) : “ Could you tell me if I’m on this Manky side
of the river or the other?”
HORSEMAN: "Can’t exactly say, mate-new hand to this part; but I think you’re on the

Dr. Loria, an Italian scientist who ha;,
spent some seven years in New Guinea, asserts.
that the Papuan woman has infinitely more power
in the village than the Papuan man. True —she ,
works in the fields, but she also owns them and,
their produce, and a man cannot touch anything
in the hut without his wife’s permission. The
“ New Woman ” in Papua is a very old institu-
tion. Last, but not least (the doctor asserts), “A.
son in New Guinea is known by his mother and!
not by his father.” A highly commendable
arrangement—for many reasons.

Orphanage Inspector Horrocks (Q.) again

But blows some good, for someone’s fill,

Once more : Why should hospital-nurses
have to pay £3 to Government before they are
allowed to stand examination for a nurse-diploma,
whereas N.S.W. Public Service Board announces
that candidates for examination for the offices of
Chief Clerk of Supreme Court and Law Clerk to
Crown Solicitor (billets worth about =£4oo a year)
will be examined for a fee of 10s. ?

A lately-divorced N.S.W. woman declares
that, previous to the Court proceedings which set
her free, her influence with members of Parliament
was so considerable that on several important occa-
sions she controlled the majority-vote.
* * * *
Our sensational little contemporary, Melb.
Argus, reported the horrible discovery, at New-
town, Sydney, of a partially devoured corpse,
under the heading “ A Cat’s Hunger.”
* * * *
The new Commissioner of Vic. Railways,
writes an irate Englishman, might as well abolish
the old rule against smoking on the platforms. It
is either a relic of the early Australian culchaw,
that perverted the proper title of the hen’s husband
into “ rooster,” or else it was invented for the use
of the wild Irish policeman who patrols the railway
terminus at Melbourne. Anyhow, the rule is
stupidly tyrannical, like many other regulations
which a down-trodden Englishman never has to
suffer, unless he leaves his free country for Scot-
land, Ireland, America, Canada, Australia, or any
other region where pubs, are closed on Sundays.
* ♦ * *
Gov. Brassey generously subscribed £5,
besides giving the prestige of his attendance at a
Northcote ecclesiastical foundation-stone laying,
the other day. Yet, throughout the festivity, the
people belonging to the concern grumbled at him
because they had spent more than the £5 for his
silver trowel. Meanwhile there was profuse cheer-
ing, bunting, and the church carpet brought out
for Lady Brassey to tread *
What most amused chose who happened
to listen to the Trinity College Mutual Admira-
tion Society’s lecture on “ Medical Education m
Melbourne lately (writes a Bulletin correspon-
dent) was the statement that, so far from hospital
patients resenting students pawing over them,
they rather liked it, and, indeed, looked forward
to it with expectation. By the way, if it is a
national necessity that medical students should
have clinical instruction, why should the privilege
of suffering for one’s bleeding country be confined
to the “ sick poor ” ? Why not devise a scheme by
which the sick friends of the Freemasons’-hall
crowd which listened to the lecture might know
the joys of being pawed ? And why doesn’t the
person who says it is so enjoyable put his own
female relatives in the way of being pawed ?
* * # *
Melb. Women’s Hospital nurses have everj
week to go on duty on two days for 12 consecutive
hours, two days for 14 hours, two days for 16
hours, and one' day for eight hours, or 92 hours a
week—practically twice as many hours a week
as a burly eight-hours unionist. This 13 hours a
day is bad enough, but not uncommonly 100 hours
a week have to be put in. There be many
sweaters in the land, but “ the greatest of these
is Charity.”
* * * *
A female, giving her name as Mary
Mahoney, was lately charged at Westminster
(Lon.) police-court with disorderly conduct. She
had kicked up an awful row at the Aquarium, and
the presiding magistrate sentenced her to 14 days
in the ordinary way of business. Whereupon a
gentleman, who is also a magistrate in his own
county, stepped forward and (rather pluckily) ad-
mitted that Mary Mahoney wasn’t any such
person, but bis own wife —in short, a disorderly
lady and not a disorderly woman. So the pri-
soner was remanded on bail for a week, after which
she was politely fined forty shillings. This story
is intended for the encouragement of wise parents
who exhort their children to be little ladies, or
little gentlemen, according to their sex. To grow
up into a plain, unvarnished man or woman is a
great mistake, for it leaves one quite at the mercy
of the law.

Bowral (N.S.W.) is a comforting place to
be buried in. The cemetery is a morass, and
when a grave is dug in wet weather the water rises
in it almost as if it was an artesian bore, and the
ghoul digs and bales turn about. At a recent,
funeral they dropped the deceased brother into
four feet of water, and he kept bobbing up all
through the service and being shoved under again,
till at last some thoughtful person got into the
grave and stood on him to hold him down. This
is why the ghosts at Bowral all have bad colds
and snuffle painfully under the trees on wet, windy
nights, and bark hollow barks among the shrub-
bery. Considering that no one really knows for
certain that a dead person hasn’t a consciousness
of his own —all we do know is that he has lost the
power of showing it, though even this is denied—
it might be more sympathetic to put him in a
dry place on the off-chance.

to denounce the New Woman, and asked i 1 a fine

bloomers ? ” Well, for that matter, do angels pass
the collection-plate, and run bazaars and tea-
meetings, and rage around for money to repair the
church-steeple ?

Someone writes to S.A. Register sug-
gesting the establishment of a People’s Hospital
in Adelaide, to be managed by the doctors at
present on strike. “ All the doctors’ old patients
would gladly return to them,” says the writer.
The “ old patients ” are evidently still uncured
then !

Melb. tradesmen want to know how it is
that Gov. Brassey, with his mighty naval influence,
cannot draw the Fleet oftener, or at all, into
Hobson’s Bay, which sadly lacks its Potts’ Point
in gusty St. Kilda.
“ What is this rum made from?” an ex-
pert was asked in a recent Melbourne liquor-case.
“ Molasses, bits of straw, maize, and the sweep-
ings of the sugar-factories, ” was the reply. Three-
penny (Melb.-made) rums are becoming very
popular at Melb. bars, and the above accounts for
the size and vigor of the nobbier.

One Northern grievance is the trans-
fer of mementoes to Brisbane museur&s. The
well-known “Explorer’s Tree,” the iron-work of
Kennedy’s carts, and the iron tank in which poor
Mrs. Watson died, are all at present peacefully
reposing in the Bananaland metropolis.

Cable news of the death and
inquesting of a young Melb. lady
in London throws some light on
the subject of “pleasure” trips
to London. The deceased spin-
ster, who sailed from Melbourne
last May, was found to have

FIRST OR LAST?
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Softly and sweetly it floated up
From the lips that his lips had nursed,
“ My hero ! you say I’m your only love,
Darling, tell me, am I th e first? ”
A sigh rippled back over memory’s sea,
And broke on the shores of the past —
“ My first, thank God, you may never be,
But pray to be ever my last.”

Currency Lass : Blair thus explains it in his “ History of
Australasia In N.S. Wales the native-born popula-
tion are distinguished as the ‘ currency ’ in opposition to
the emigrant portion of the inhabitants, who are (or for-
merly were) considered as ‘ sterling.’ These terms
originated in the difference which, at one period, some of
the financial arrangements of the colonial Govt, had occa-
sioned between the nominal value of the £ sterling and
its real worth as an article of currency—the latter then
being considerably lower than its proper and standard
estimate.” Hence, currency lass —a native-born girl

Mr Dear Moorabinda—
X achieved a seat at the last “ Chinatown ”
morning performance last Wednesday at Sydney
Lyceum, and was not surprised to find that, owing
t? th ®,? ewi ßering sartorial abbreviations which
the Chinatown ladies superstitiously regard as
skirts in act 3, the wear and tear of a Sydney
season had left their elevated hems unscathed. I
hope Patrice, as the “dude,” will not start Aus-
tralian Johnnydom sporting soup-plate nosegays
again, through her effective trick of crowding a
whole cauliflower-head, apparently, into her but-
ton-hole. This kind of thing is good and worthy
’ n a mufoal trifle,” but when it comes to Reggie
de Tonbob doing the Block trimmed with four
sunflowers and a cabbage-rose, the thing is likely
to bring about a tax on button-holes. Which
reminds me that the P. of W. can’t possibly wear
coat-flowers, or else they never would have disap-
peared with such a rush. Nobody seems to wear
a button-holer now unless he is going to be mar-
ried, or in some equally irresponsible state of
mind. At balls now a button-holer is almost as
vulgar as a red silk handkerchief negligently
thrust into the dress weskit. J

office clearly enough during the average airy per-

! ” There ’s a lesson in a nutshell

Women vvho hugger-mugger little sooty kittens
should be interested in the revelations at the
inquest on a woman found dead in a Sydney
suburb the other day. She, poor soul, lived alone
with a cherished black cat, and, when her body
wa,s discovered, puss was sitting on the pillow
licking her lips—and half her mistress’s face was
eaten away ! Isn’t it consoling to know that the
purring lump of fur which nestles so confidingly on
your shoulder has all that up its sleeve should the
occasion arise ! Ugh !
After all, there’s in “ animals ” very little senti-
ment—as well-fed humans understand the word,
borne years ago, a great outcry was made over the
inhumanity of a trooper, in the Mount Browne
district of N.S.W., who shot a faithful dog which
he had found beside the body of its dead master,
on a thirsty track. The dog had so obstinately
refused to allow the constable to approach the
poor bushman s cold clay that nothing remained
but to kill it, explained the officer. This explana-
tion was, however, deemed quite insufficient,
whereupon the trooper added, by way of post-
senpt: And I have further to report that the
l,° g rn , P artlal] y eaten deceased.” Whereupon
the lobyphiles shut up with a loud click.

Songs and waltzes come to me this week in the
proportion of one song to five waltzes. The song,
Ihe Daylight is Dying,” words by “Banjo”
1 atterson and music by Rene Goring Thomas
published by Angus and Robertson, is not over-
effective, but one of those prattling semi-tunes
winch neither irritate nor interest you and which
leave little impression. It is written for con-
S The waltzes (from Paling & Co.) are no
If!?. than the bulk of local dance-music. The
Ilka waltz by Frances E. Walsh, and dedi-
cated to Dr. Allaster Cox, has rather a reckless
air, but it is so peppered with semi-quavers and
grace notes that it seems to fairly hiccough The
Antonia ” waltz, by Mary Maguire (the lady has
other names, but no composer is allowed to have
more than two or three in The Bulletin) is not so
p tty as her Katoomba ” waltz, and has not the
advantage of so striking a title-page, but the
rhythm is good all through. The “ Zenda ” waltz,
by Emilie Ksepper, has a fairly fresh first move-
ment, but gets weak and spidery to conclude, and
the (inevitable !) Cyclist’s ” waltz, by A. Curtin
suggests bumps and bruises and punctured tyres*
and is a perfunctory outburst obviously tinkered
together “ to meet the times. ”

Glen and Co., of Melbourne and Sydney, are
publishing a “ Trilby Gavotte ” and an “Isabella
Gavotte,” both of fair average merit. The first-
named is embellished with a photo, of Maggie
Moore calling attention to her pretty feet, and a
copy of “Ben Bolt” is thrown into the bargain.
Another up-to-date issue from the same firm is
‘Molly’s the Girl for Me,” the tuneful ditty sung
m “ A Trip to Chinatown.”
Writes my Melb. correspondent:—
“A subscription-ball run by the Govt. House
people proved irresistible, and even the meanest of
the mean forked out their 12s. 6d. to buy a ticket
from His Ex.’s daughter or an English lord.
The ball was very well done in every way, and
was absolutely crammed with members of the
upper-crust and would-be society folk; in fact,
Prahran Town Hall could well have been twice as
Bright Mrs. Freeman-Thomas “ran the
show,” from designing the very swell decorations,
all red drapery, deer and fox heads, mirrors, and
saddles, stirrups, whips, bridles, &c., &c., to
marching young men up in autocratic fashion
and presenting them to the ladies. The
gowns were gorgeous, and powder and patches
made pretty women look prettier, and alas ! plain
women plainer. All the vice-regal woinenkind
were poudre, and sported the latest London fad :
a black velvet band round the throat, of course in
their case covered with diamonds. Champagne,
&c., said to be provided by Mrs. Freeman-Thomas
and Mrs. Everard Brown, simply flowed, as was
convincingly proved by the unsteady gait of some
of the men towards the end of the evening.
''The Govt. House hop could not compare for
brilliancy with the Hunt Club ball; indeed, the
former was not quite so grand an affair as the pre-
vious gathering at the stucco mansion. There
was nothing very particular about the affair ex-
cepting some exceedingly swell gowns and plenty
of diamonds. Lady B. was fairly ablaze. The
only innovation was the apparatus for warming
the room, i.e., a large asbestos stove among the
ferns on either side of the dais, which soon made
the atmosphere too hot, and doors and windows
perforce were opened, letting in uncomfortable
blasts of frosty air. Supper was on from 11 o’clock,
and it was quite a sight to see crowds of uni-
formed men and bejewelled dames waiting for
half-an-hour at a time like hungry animals at the
barrier. H.E. wore white satin knee-breeches
and much gold-laced coat, and the warbling earl,
•i° i las burned from the country, delighted the
girls by appearing in his gorgeous uniform.
“ There has been great excitement below stairs
in local mansions whose mistresses had re-
ceived invitations from Guv’ment ’Ouse for a
man and maid to be present at the vice-regal ser-
vants’ ball. Of course, these invitations caused
much heart-burning, for many a maid had to go
with another girl’s flame, her own not being a gen-
tleman’s gentleman or house-servant.
. An ultra-smart ‘ society ’ journal surpassed all
its previous efforts recently by publishing a full
account of a wedding just five days before the
ceremony took place, causing no end of embarrass-
ment to the fair bride.
Yours affectionately,
SAPHO SUITH.
SPORTING NOTIONS.
Sundry English professionals engaged to play
against the Australians last Monday struck on Friday
for higher wages, and Sydney Telegraph remarked
that the proceeding, though right in itself, was “ inoppor-
tune —they ought to have struck a week earlier. Natu-
rally they should. Then the cricket authorities would
have had 10 days in which to fill their places, and their
chance of success would have amounted to about as much
as the chance of the emu habitually taking to paper
collars. The principal boss of Sydney D.T. is also chair-
man of the ’bus co., and, on this occasion, the paper was
possibly thinking of a ’bus-drivers’ strike and the
expediency of same being so conducted as to give the boss
10 days or more to fill the vacancies. The other people
think that the right time to strike is when there is a
chance of success, and that time is when the boss person
needs them badly and hasn’t time to replace them. If
they don’t strike then, what is the good of doing it at all?
A general doesn’t usually give the enemy 10 days’notice
that he is going l to attack ; he comes along in the most
beastly inopportune way he can, and the man who is
most inopportune is the best general. A strike is indus-
trial war, and the art of being inopportune is the only
thing about strike-management worth knowing.
Editor Bulletin. —Will you allow me, the
cricket scribe who “ tries to excuse English defeats,” and
who comments on the comparative slowness of Australian
batting, to inform your angry correspondent that I argue
from figures, not prejudice. Your A.C’s had better study
this season s records of inter-county matches in England
and thereby convince themselves that the counties 1 ! as a
rule, score much faster against one another than the Aus-
tralians score against them. Your correspondent, “ Aus-
tralian,” cites three games out of some 20 odd in’which
the English batting has seemed to be slow. Of these
three games, that between Notts and Australia was the
only one commenced on a good wicket, and Notts—fre-
quently instanced by The Bulletin as the dullest of
English batting elevens—played with poor spirit cer-
tainly. As regards the cable that “ Surrey took all day
making 283 runs, whereas the Australians scored 376 for
their day,” what are the facts of the case ? The wicket
when Surrey opened the match was “heavy, owing to
recent rains.” So recent were the rains that play had to
be postponed for a time. The score at lunch-time indi-
cated as much to anyone not blinded by patriotic preju-
dice. Next day the wicket must have recovered from the
dampness, or the cable would have stated otherwise. The
cable reports are like your correspondent, “ Australian ”
One word more. Will “ Australian ” bet long odds that
Trott didn’t actually win the toss in that match and send
Surrey in first to bat upon the dead wicket? I don’t
think he would care to make a wager. Better to wait and
see when full details come to hand. And if Trott didn’t
win the toss against Surrey, it was the only time he has
lost it, out of eleven successive spins, up to the time of
writing. The good luck of the Australians throughout
then present tour hus, I lntiiiitiiin, been not less rem&rk-
able than their good play.— The Culprit Scribe.

The inclusion of Prince Ranji in All-England
elevens has a political appearance. Ranji, not beinv a
bowler, is by no means indispensable. He’s a silken bond
between India and the dear old foster-mother country.

undoubtedly caught by Hayward in the first test-match
though the umpire decided in his favor.

Phil. Greenwood, who went ’Ome with Harry
Musgrove and “ Our Boys,” wasn’t much struck by the
improvements in London since he left it about 15 years
ago. Birmingham, Manchester, and other manufacturing
towns are dirtier than ever ; so he will be glad to get back
to Australia. Long association with the Firm’s theatrical
business has blunted the tall young man’s appetite for
nee admission to the’alls and other places which seen]
heavenly to young members of the team.

* * * *
A match between Warwickshire and Leicester-
shire counties, played in June, gives a fair idea of their
inferiority to the Australians. The two teams finished
neck and neck, Warwickshire making 477 altogether, and
Leicestershire pipping them when the last man in had
knocked up an unexpected 46. Three individual scores
of a century” were made, two of them by Warwick
shire smiters, though Woodcock, bowling for the winners
had a total average of 11 wickets for 141. Neither county
showed any such brilliant performances against the Aus-
tralians, who beat them both easily.
When The Bulletin went to press the third
test-match had been practically left to be decided on the
second innings. The English won the toss, and the first
wicket (Grace s) fell at 54. The score stood at 69 for one
wicket when stumps were drawn on the first day (play
did not begin till late, owing to rain); yet the innings
closed for 145. The Australians did even worse, for after
losing one wicket (Iredale’s) for 75, all were out for 119
ihe hero of the occasion was Hearne, who clean-bowled
five wickets for next to no runs.
* * * *
Sussex may get a tidy score against Australia,
with the aid of the two foreigners, Kanji and Murdoch,
also Bean and others. But the county bowling is not
strong enough to bring off a victory under ordinary
circumstances. Now that Surrey has failed to get rid of
Irott & Co. for a small sum, the latter seem invincible in
district matches.

* * * *
A new tyre has been patented in Melbourne in
which the inner tube is divided into a number of separate
sections each about an inch long. Claimed that in the
event of two or three sections puncturing the tyre will
still be rideable. A price was offered locally for the patent
rights, but the inventor talks of taking it ’Ome to the
Dunlop Co., who have probably been working the same
line of idea. The section-tyre scheme is a first cousin to
the nautical system of water-tight compartments.
* * * *
The Rover people were not long matching
Megson and Co. against a team representing an opposi-
tion firm,, but the deal was a failure. On the first day
the,v didn t secure a single place, while, on the second
day, Ernie Payne managed to struggle into third place in
each event. As Parlbv, who won all the events, is not a
.top-notcher, it would at first appear that the Australians
were clean outclassed ; but a more feasible explanation is
that they were not in their best form, as shown by the
position occupied by Lewis —last —in a slow-run five miles.
A letter from Payne shows that they cannot have been
more than four or five weeks in training.
The wheel will hum at Sydney Ag. Ground the
next two Saturdays. Among the crouchers on the pedals
who will win or scorch themselves blind are “Bill”
Martin, who has come back laden with spoils from the
barbarous North ; A. B. M‘Donell, another famous
Murkan; P-ither, Boyd, Ken. Lewis, Middleton, Clinton,
M Lean, Porta, the speedy and rising Meadham, and other
representative stars. Also, Professor Marschner, the Ger-
man trick-rider, will, after performing many surprising
feats, take his wheel to pieces bit by bit and at last pedal
on the air. N.B.—Guaranteed authentic.

• * * *
Not a few Sydney tote-shops are booming ahead
—advertising straight-out backing, 2s. 6d. and ss. tote,
and doubles—under a false sense of security. The fact
that they have not lately been molested by the police
must not be taken as a guarantee for the future. As a
matter of fact, it is hinted in official circles that a big
swoop may be shortly expected, and that conviction will
mostly mean gaol. And the raid will probably not follow
any previous intimation to shut up under pain of arrest,
but will take place simultaneously at almost every recog-
nised tote-shop in town.

THE FRASER RAILWAY AUTOMATIC
DOOR-CONTROLLER.
THE LATEST AUSTRALIAN INVENTION.
As Mr. R. A. Fraser is proceeding to London via
America in a few days for the purpose of introduc-
ing his valuable invention to the administrators of
the English and continental railways, a brief des-
cription of the appliance may prove interesting to
Bulletin readers. The chief points which Mr.
Fraser claims for his invention are the following :
1. Economy : When the system is introduced the
present 3taff of suburban or platform porters will
be reduced to a minimum. [The late Chairman of
the Victorian Railways Commission estimated
that the yearly saving in this direction in the
province of Victoria would amount to from .£25,000
to .£30,000 on the suburban lines alone.] 2. The
saving of time : All the doors of a train of carriages
being opened and closed simultaneously. 3. The
saving of compensation for crushed limbs and
other accidents incidental to the old system. 4.
Absolute safety to the travelling public, and
largely increased convenience. The invention,
controlled by the guard from his van, is worked by
compressed air. The guard is able to manipulate
all the doors of all the carriages composing a train.
He can open and shut them, latch or unlatch
them, and lock or unlook them, and can also indi-
cate to the passengers the name of the station
which the train may be approaching. The dura-
tion of stoppages, and the names of the stations
intersecting with branch lines, may also be shown
on the indicators, to which the attention of the
passengers is drawn by a gong. The gong is also
sounded when the doors are opened, closed, or
locked. Trains may travel with the doors latched
and locked, or unlocked, as may be desired. #

Primate Smith acts so faithfully up to his
advice to boys—“ Don’t read newspapers ” —that
his journalistic excursions in a whole week seldom
occupy more than half-an-hour.
Carington and Countess were lately
summoned at Wycombe (Bucks) Sessions for riding
unlighted bicycles after duskfall, and fined nix,
with ss. 9d. costs in each case. The ex-Lord Cham-
berlain bailed up on a bike ! The knell of all
human majesty is sounded.
Though the West coast miners of Tasmania
tolerate no Chinese on the field, the man who
would, perhaps, poll highest for any office the
Tas. miners have to bestow is Tom Bak Hap, an
abnormally brainy half-caste Chinaman, whose
pen-name, by the way, is not unknown to
Bulletin readers.
Sydney parson Rutledge, who looms un-
naturally big at present, owing to anti-liquor
atmospheric disturbances, professes to be the
lineal descendant of a never-touch-it signer of the
American declaration of independence. The
hostility to liquor was transmitted, but the hosti-
lity to monarchy died with the signer.
H.—“ Add J. H. Brooke to the Australians
in Japan. It was a wonder why he went —some
huff, in the midst of his Victorian political fame.
He was the pioneer, with his Occupation Licenses,
as Lands Minister, of the work oarried forward by
J. M. Grant. A great career would have been
Brooke’s, had he remained.
Dr. Cockbum, Minister of Education
(S.A.), has had a severe illness, during which he
was attended by one of the newly-imported Ade-
laide Hospital doctors. Folk are now wondering
what would have happened to him had no medical
talent been available beyond that of his enemies—
the general practitioners now u “ strike.”

of Miss Le Breton. Lily was one of the brides-

young wife caught cold and, later, died of con-
sumption. Twelve months after Mr. Langtry re-
turned to St. Heliers and married Miss Le Breton,
who at that time was far from being noticeably
beautiful; she was scraggy.
At a Melb. suburban police-court the other
day Helen Troy was sent up for vagrancy. The
prosecuting sergeant aptly referred to her as a
Parisibe !
Premier Forrest (W.A.) will be 49 August
22, Education-Minister Cockburn (S.A.) will be 46
August 23, and Purves, Q.C., will be 53 on the
same date.
Maoriland obituary : Cunningham, of
Christchurch, erstwhile a very big grain-dealer.
An unusual kind of trader, too, for when, a la
Duke of Plaza Toro, he had formed himself into a
limited co., he made up to the shareholders
of losses, without being under any obligation to
do so. Such men deserve the honor of dying poor
—and do.
The original warrant for the execution of
Sir Henry Browne Hayes, the first knight who
came to N.S. Wales, is in the possession of the
secretary for Rockhampton (Q.) School of Arts.
Hayes was sentenced to death at Cork assizes m
Aug. 1801, for the forcible abduction of an heiress
named Mary Pike, but the sentence was commuted
to life-banishment to Botany Bay.
By the recent election of Dr. Cullen to the
vacant seat in Sydney University Senate, that
academical body now numbers .nine barristers.
The total strength of the Senate is 20, so that the
wigged wing of the Devil’s Brigade makes a brave
show. The Six-and -Eight-Penny wing is wholly
unrepresented : no solicitor has appeared on the
roll since the days of Wigram Allen.

John Plummer, whose industry as the
“ Voice of the People,” “Pro Bono Publico,” and
“ Citizen,” has not been recently called upon by
the N.S.W. country papers, owing, presumably,
to the paucity of funds belonging to the National
Ass, is literary henchman to the N.S.W. Railway
Commissioners. The recent arrival of the Co-
operative delegates in Australia is a great feather
in Plummer’s cap, as the visit is partly
due to his indefatigable pegging away in the
English Co-Operative News. Plummer began
his literary career when a weaver working at the
loom, and for years acted as London correspondent
of the S.M. Herald.

Mr. B. A. FRASER, Melb., inventor of the Fraser
Railway Automatic Door Controller.
Sydney Bankruptcy Court item, under
“ Public Examinations” Be Armand Jerome.
Bankrupt did not appear, and the examination
was adjourned sine die.” No, he does not appear.
Mr. R. A. Fraser, of Melb., whose portrait
is published herewith, appears fated to win
“wealth with honor” as the inventor of the
railway automatic door-controller which bears his
name. His evolution from bank-clerk to success-
ful mechanical inventor is sufficiently singular to
attach interest to the personality of the man who
seems about to scale Fortune’s ladder at the early
age of 28. It was Mr. Fraser’s father . who
secured the initial success of antimony mining
many years ago at Costerfield (Vic.), and much of
his enthusiasm and industry has obviously de-
scended to his son. Fraser fils was educated at
Btawell (Vic.) grammar school, and at the age of
18 entered the service of the Bank of Victoria in
that town. After six years’ experience of ledgers
and overdrafts the weary clerkling retired from the
bank, in order to devote himself fully to his in-
vention. From the germ of the idea, which
originated 10 years ago, Mr. Fraser has now per-
fected his unique patent. He proceeds to London
in a few days, with every hope of securing its
adoption on the British and continental railways.
The accompanying illustrations depict the
latest success in bicycle development. The improve-
ment of the driving mechanism of a bicycle is a
problem on which much mechanical genius has
been focussed. And the “Metropole,” by the
substitution of cog-wheels and shaft for chain gear,
claims to have made a very long step for-
ward. Mr. Eden, the well-known Sydney
photographer, is “ Metro pole ” agent for
all Australia, bar S.A. and Westralia. He
points out that the mechanism, being of the
finest steel, and preserved in a hermetic-
ally-sealed case, will last several years,
whereas the chain-gear has to be replaced
every 12 months. The Military and Post
and Telegraph Departments of the French
Government adopted the Acatene Metro-
pole because of its strength, rigidity, light-
ness and speed. The manufactory employs
3000 hands, and its existence is a boom.
Rivffire, the French crack, rode a Metro-
pole when he ran second to Linton in the
367-mile race from Bordeaux to Paris. Mr.
Eden hasasamplemachineathis studio, and
is booking orders daily for expected ship-
ments, which will arrive a few weeks hence.

MATTERS MELBOURNIAN.


The “Pleasant 'Sun-day Afternoon” at
the Centenary-hall yesterday took the Jdrm of a
meeting for men only, at which the* speakers were
the Rev. Dr. Porter (of Petershaml, the Rev. X.
Woodhouse, and Dr. Arthur. The Rev. W. Woolls
Rutledge presided.
The chairman, In a short address, referred to
the apathy of Governments in matters of social
purity. He said it might noddle generally known,
.but there existed at the Technical College'a class
fpr the study of the nude. This class was founded
by ;a late Minister, for Education; but they had
gone to see the present Government on the subject
and a report was called for.. The report stated
that the figure Was appropriately draped, yet he
had been told that on one occasion at least the ,
figure was naked. This was one of the things "
which the Government should put down. ’ The
class was attended by young men and wotpen, and
the subject for the payment of a few shillipgs
stood and allowed herself to be sketched. HA
'protested against this.
