POLITICAL
POINTS
Be N.S.W. Protectionist leadership—
Toby or not Toby, that is the question !
“ Woman Suffrage, the last resource of a
Bankrupt Democracy,’’says Canon Berry, of Melb.
Good phrase, anyhow.
Forty fresh names have just been added
to the N.S.W. pension-list, the amounts payable
running from £2B to £545 per annum.
After the recent Brunswick (Vic.) Orange-
Green riots several large employers on both sides
dismissed a number of their hands on account of
their “color.”
Westralia’s chief inspector of stock : “We
have not now a single scabby sheep.” The inspec-
tor, of course, meant four-footed animals, and
wasn’t thinking of the local Mines Department.
SENTIMENT UNDER DIFFICULTIES.
SHE: “ What! don’t you love me in my bicycle
suit? ”
HE: “ Oh, it’s all right now—but at first it was a
bit like kissing a fellow."
The new Vic. United Labor-party very
rightly recognises that the referendum is a corol-
lary of elective Ministries, and finds prominent
places for both these issues in its announced
policy.
In British Parliamentary papers up to
date, imports from British colonies have always
been classed among “foreign,” but for “silken
bonds” purposes they are in future to be classed
by themselves.
The story goes that a N.S.W. Minister,
who has had a State bedroom fitted up for himself
in his departmental offices, sleeps luxuriously
there between sheets purchased with the people’s
money at £2 12s. per pair.
Parties are so evenly balanced in S. A. Leg.
Council that the Govt, finds great difficulty in
passing liberal measures. Next April there will
be eight retirements. Big political events depend
upon the voting on that occasion.
One member of N.S.W. Parliament is said
to own five public-houses. Although local option
ranks as a question of public policy, it is indecent
for a man who owns even one public-house to
speak or vote in Parliament against any form of
liquor restriction bill.
King O’Malley, M.P., is making heroic
endeavors to be taken seriously by S.A. people,
press, and politicians, but so far his presence in
Parliament is looked upon as a huge practical
joke. It is satisfactory to know that his vote is
always given on the Radical side.
The Reid Govt, has finally decided that no
special consideration can be extended to the
N.S.W. sugar-growers, and the duty will come off
by instalments as already arranged. As a Free-
trade Govt, it couldn’t do anything else; so an-
other big industry is taking a bee-line for Tophet.
Sydney D.T. says now that, three months
ago, it pointed out that the test year of the N.S.
Wales Freetrade Treasurer’s finance would be
1896-7, and could not by any possibility be taken
as 1895-6. Then why did the D.T., with all the
other local Foreign-trade organs, whoop hifalutins
on Reid’s alleged surplus three or four weeks ago?
The Victorian conference of trades and
labor organisations has made “ the election of
Ministers by Parliament ” a plank in the Labor
platform of the Cabbage Garden. Another of its
planks is “the abolition of the Legislative Council.”
This last is as yet so debateable a question among
Labor’s own friends that it will seriously militate
against the acceptance of the Vic. Labor-policy as
a whole.
Caldwell, M.L.A., of South Australia : “I
believe the best body to govern this colony would
be one, of which half was chosen by the British
bondholders, and the other half elected to repre-
sent the best interests of the colony.” The best
interests of S.A., according to Caldwell, are, of
course, sheep, boiling-down establishments, land-
jobs and land-grant shyndikits. The best in-
terests ! !
Old Jock M‘lntyre, Vic. Opposition leader,
has an idea for squaring the finances. If the rail-
way deficit was out of the way there would be a
big surplus. Therefore, MTntyre proposes that
the railway deficit be simply added to the public
debt every year, and that the province shall then
proceed to have a real good time. The Australian
financiers who see their way to do something
great by whooping up the public debt are too
many to count.
Tasmania really doesn’t know this year
whether its Parliament is open or not. When the
Governor went through the usual ceremony he
quite forgot to wind up with the usual remark:
“I now declare this Parliament open,” and deep
legal persons with horse-hair wigs are somewhat
worried about the situation. If the Gov. didn’t
really open Parliament, can it be open ? And if
it can be open without him opening it, then what
is the use of him opening it at all ?
Raid’s latest charge against the Protec-
tionists is copied from a very old stage device of
Parkes, and is labelled—“ Slandering the fair
fame of this country in the eyes of the world. ”
As nobody cares particularly about the fair fame
of this country except the British money-lender,
the real inwardness of the charge—also of the
alleged surplus— must be to facilitate a loan in the
usual quarters.
When Premier Reid rounds on
ex-Treasurer M‘Millan in terms of
fine scorn for base betrayal of his
party, and for harassing their flanks
with alarums and incursions, he
seems to forget that some four short
summers ago he stood in precisely
the same position towards Parkes
and M‘Millan. Then he worried
them from behind, and rounded
them up, and bustled them. Now
the perky, ready M‘Millan has re-
versed the position of affairs, and
seems determined to avenge himself
and his dead chief by hovering
around and cutting-off all stragglers,
burning Reid’s bridges, and confis-
cating his surpluses. It was Parkes’
legacy to M‘Mi]lan ; and, whatever
may be said of the death-bed re-
conciliation of Reid and Parkes,
the old warrior’s taunt still lives:
“ If they wanted another captain,
there was no necessity to put a
pirate in my place ! ”
It reads curiously in the Syd-
ney shipping reports: “July 24,
Fifeshire, steamer, from London, 62
bags corn, 1200 packages flour,” &c.
Again, ‘ ‘ Bungaree, steamer, from
London, 934 bags flour.” And then,
“ Indraghiri, steamer, from Lon-
don, 1754 bags flour.” Under the
festering influence of Foreign-trade
it actually seems that the great,
rich, new country of N.S.W. is
beginning to depend on England for the com-
monest varieties of agricultural produce.
The shrewd old miner known to history,
who listened to the debates of eminent theologians
and always knew who got the best of it, because
the other fellow lost his temper, would have had
no difficulty, the other evening, in discovering the
winner of the Reid-M‘Millan controversy if he
had watched the former tearing passion to tatters,
while Mac sat with a still, cold smile, a pale, calm
face, and arms folded over his breast —after having
demolished the Reidian system of finance.
Murray Smith, M.P., Vic. : “I distinctly
state it is not the duty of a Government to find
work for the people. It is the people that support
the Government.” Logic ! It is the duty of a
Government to do what the people darn well
please, since the Government is merely an instru-
ment of the people, but the pity is that people like
Mr. Smith have not been made to see that, because
of the weakness of the people in continuing to
support insupportable Governments. Smith is a
living proof of the proverb, “Everything is pos-
sible in a Democracy.”
Some S.A. Tories, in view of Way’s pos-
sible Privy Councillorship, are howling at the
prospect of Kingston succeeding him as Chief
Justice. Such a contingency is looked upon as
impossible by all who know the ambitious S.A.
Premier, who gave up a big legal practice for
politics, and would never sacrifice his political
prospects for the chill monotony of the Bench.
Yic. Legislative Council is
thinking of referring the Companies
Bill to a select committee which will
take the evidence of experts. This
will cut two ways. The experts are
the people who run companies or
manage or audit them, and as the bill
is to block all their profitable swin-
dles they will necessarily be against
it, and their evidence will furnish an
excuse for dropping the whole busi-
ness. Moreover, the experts will give
evidence for so many weeks that the
end of the session will probably come
while they are still at it, and then
Sargood and Co. will go to their con-
stituents and tell them that they
haven’t rejected the bill, but are stiff
toiling at it to make it perfect, and the
constituents will be so much pleased
that they will re-elect Sargood forth-
with.
R. T. Yale’s resignation from
the Turner (Vic.) Ministry is not to
be wondered at, seeing that he held
a payless portfolio, and had done
much of the Ministerial bullocking.
He was head and front of the com-
mittee that inquired minutely into
the working of departments, seeking
victims for the retrenchment knife,
and sniffing out extravagance and
abuses. He had a keen scent for
abuses, and did valuable Democratic
work, facing the opprobrium of the
“curled darlings,” and making
enemies of the right sort. The Govt,
has not grit to back him up, and
abolish the evils he exposed, so he
steps out, having done for nothing, in
one way or another, work for which
two Ministerial screws would only
have been a reasonable reward. Vale
is mostly real grit, only his close
association with the professional tea-
swillers and social-purity gad-abouts
keeps him from going up top in the
Vic. Radical party. The Demo-
cracy mistrusts a man when he has
got the anti-beer habit so bad that
his weakness verges on dipso-
mania.
The Adelaide R.O. clergy, alarmed at the
liberal political lines on which the Southern
Cross, their denominational organ, has been con-
ducted by managing-director O’Loghlin (Chief
Secretary), at the last half-yearly meeting suc-
ceeded in getting three priests and a sympathetic
layman elected on a board of six members to
modify the tone of the weekly—which, by the
way, on the year’s transactions paid 20. per cent,
div. Lands-Minister Gillen is the sixth director.
The situation of the Bank of N.Z. in-
quiry, now being held at Wellington, is about
this : The Opposition wants to inquire into all
matters for about two years back so as to expose
the iniquitous wav ex-Treasurer Ward or his
Association let in the Colonial Bank. The Sed-
don Government wants to carry the inquiry back
to ’BB in order to expose also the way some promi-
nent Oppositionists, who are now dreadfully
shocked about Ward, let in the B.N.Z. for enor-
mous sums which were surreptitiously wiped off,
and which have been kept dark to this day. And
each Potts says that the other Slurk is trying to
smother investigation.
GOOD SALTING MATERIAL .
(A Tale of the Wild West.)
JACK : “ Gray and Yensen sold their claim to them newchums
for a decent figure; hadn’t you a better show?”
BILL : “ No. You see, Yensen’s false teeth was mounted with
gold, mine’s just celluloid.”
Been There: There is proportionately more
grog consumed in Mildura and Renmark, Aus-
tralia’s ‘ ‘ temperance ” colonies, than in the majority
of towns where licensed hotels flourish. And what
vile stuff some of the alleged whisky is, especially
that bought at the Renmark shanties ! One result
of the prohibition of open sale is that the whole of
the population seem sly and snaky, suspicious of
each other’s every action. The people are almost
unanimous in condemning the prohibition, but a
small minority obstructs alteration of the law.
Premier Reid has evidently been a close
student of Parkes’ political tricks. Success has
followed some of the weary old platitudes by
which “ national ” interests are assured on
their annual “ day out ” that he is with
them, though not of them —at an English affair
that he is proud of belonging to the great hempire
(Reid aspirates the “ h ”); at an Irish meeting
that if he hadn’t been something else he would
have had no hesitation in being proud of being an
Irishman ; and at a Scotch festival that an ances-
tress had a narrow escape of being married to
Bobby Burns.
Judge Williams (Vic.) points out that at
the conference of Judges held in Melbourne at the
time of the great Exhibition, he tabled a proposal
for the establishment of a Federal Court of Ap-
peal. Higinbotham, Way, and Boucaut ap-
plauded and supported the motion, but the Sydney
Judges were fierce in opposition, and the oppor-
tunity and the motion were lost.
A FRACTION
The Girl in Background (as the first couple hurry past)-.
“ Who is that with old Major Got dam ' ”
THE MAN : “ Oh, he married lately; that’s his better half,
you know.”
Anomalous that while the N.S.W. At-
torney-General may perform as much private
practice as he can get hold of, his secretary is not
permitted to do so. Is the secretary to the
Attorney-General, then, a “civil servant”? No.
There is no valid reason for his being differentiated
in this respect from the Attorney-General; it is
merely the glorifying habit of “ greasing the fat
sow.” The Attorney-General should not be per-
mitted to earn extra-official money by private
practioe, but then the Attorney-General should
not be a political office-holder at all, but a paid,
permanent servant of the State.
Premier Reid, in N.S.W. Assembly : “In
1892, when the Dibbs Government was in power
and spent <£11,000,000 of public money,” &c., &c.
According to Coghlan’s Statistical Register
(page 328) the net expenditure of ’92 was
■£9,931,411. So, unless Coghlan is very far astray,
Premier Reid stands convicted of meanly manu-
facturing figures and falsifying statistics to dis-
credit his predecessors. In fact, almost every time
G. Reid comes along with a batch of figures he is
bowled out in glaring and childish misrepresenta-
tions. Yet he comes along with the next batch
quite cheerfully as an individual whose accuracy is
beyond question.
The Queensland Boodle Government is
running the Federation question on Fat Man
principles all round. Not only has it omitted
from the Enabling Bill the provision that the
people are to elect the Convention delegates, but
also the clause providing that the proposed Federal
Constitution is to be accepted or rejected by a
popular vote. Consequently the Boodle Ministry
gets there in two separate ways. Its servile Par-
liamentary majority elects the delegates who will
help to draw up the Constitution. And the same
servile majority will accept or reject the Constitu-
tion when it is drawn up—will accept it if it is so
brutally Tory that it wouldn’t be safe to submit it
to a popular vote because the people would throw
it out, and reject it if it is so Liberal that there is
any chance of the people wanting to accept it. So
the people don’t get a look-in either way.
The N.S.W. railway-revenue for the year
just ended was <£109,000 short of working-expenses
and interest on the whole cost of construction.
The Vic. railway-deficit was nearly £600,000, and
the distracted Melb. legislator wants to know the
reason why.. Which reason sticks out so conspicu-
ously that his inability to see it is simply wonder-
ful. Vic. has four times more railway than N.S.W.
in proportion to area, and 25 per cent, more in
proportion to population—in fact, Vic. is a dread-
fully over-railwayed province. Moreover, things
are so arranged that nearly two-thirds of the
province hasn’t the semblance of a line anywhere,
while in the other third you can’t walk two miles
without stubbing your toe against a railway.
The railways are jammed together in the centre of
the province, and the rest of it is a howling wil-
derness.


Last N.A. Review points out that the
building-regulations of Paris, Berlin, and Vienna
prohibit sky-palaces in those cities, such as are
familiar in America and Australia. Not only is
height limited definitely, but it can never be more
than the breadth of the street.
Injections of blood-serum taken from an
ordinary healthy horse act as a most powerful
tonic ; the red corpuscles rapidly multiply, weight
is gained, and the patient becomes visibly stronger
daily. It is especially valuable in disorders of
nutrition and nervous troubles like St. Vitus
dance.

A new cancer-remedy is being tried with
good results in Victoria—a solution of dye called
methylene-blue, injected and swallowed. Itseenis |
to act as a poison to the cancer microbe.
The Australian branches of the United
Type Writer and Supplies Co. are steadily reaping
a deserved reward with the Caligraph No. 4, Yost,
and Densmore typewriters. These machines gain
more and more public favor as Australians wake
up to the fact that to save time is to gain time.
The Westralian Governor has recently purchased
a Densmore for private use ; also a Yost. Call-
graph No. 4 being the improved model of the popu-
lar machine is testimonialized far and wide.*

Melbourne suburban newspapers seem to
be dying off gradually. Job-printing, which has
practically kept them alive for alo g time, is row

even “ old-established ” papers are hanging on by

Dyspepsia and other mal-nutrition troubles
are being successfully treated in Europe by means
of electricity, one electrode being placed against
the pit of the stomach and the other inserted by
the mouth into the stomach itself, after the organ
has been half-filled with water.

* * * *
Zouroff would certainly have carried The
Bulletin s prediction into second or third place for
Caulfield G.N Steeplechase but for his blunder at the
last jump. The race at that point was confined to
Larrikin and Zouroff, the former appearing master of
the situation. When his nearest rival came to grief
Glasscock s horse was left with a very long lead, and he
cantered down the straight 30 lengths in front of old
Cerberus, who was about half as far ahead of the weary
Banjo. Larrikin, starting at “fives,” had been a street-
corner cert, during the week, and the public received him
graciously.
. .* * * *
Larrikin made record time for the V.A.T.C.
National Steeplechase, 4) seconds less than Daimio
registered last year with 321 b. more in the saddle
Daimio s, by the way, was 17 seconds faster than the
previous record, and yet the Hinglish critics are said to
sneer at the Australian champion. We shall see what we
shall observe in the cablegrams a few months hence
* * * *
Larrikin, Saturday s Oaulfield victor, has been
racing into form during the past month or so. The field
numbered 13, as usual. The winner, a descendant of
Panic, put up a record of B.ll£ for the 4 miles.
* * * *
S.M. Herald contends that Caulfield Steeple
should be reduced from 4 to 3 miles, on account of the
accidents. Its own report shows that the casualties
started at a mile and a-half. Yet the going was good and
the afternoon long.

The match between Dr. Fitzgerald’s horse Huon
Junior and Mr. “Jack” Samuel’s mare Hattie S., for £2OO
*-side,has quite livened up trotting matters about Sydnev.
Hattie is one of the dozy sort, and needs a lot of waking
up, but she stands the whalebone like a ’bus horse, and is
very speedy when roused. The match eventuates at
Brighton 18th Sept., mile heats.

Malwa and Madge Gordon, the best of the
bunch of saplings that started for the first flutter at
Oaulfield, were easily beaten by Emblazon last Saturday
This time the journey was 3£ furlongs, and the extra
110 yarns, perhaps, overtaxed their “staying power”
Malwa, certainly, carried a stone penalty for winning o ‘n
the first day, but 141 b. over such a course ought noit to
trouble even a young two-year-old.
* * * *
A travelling trotter raced “nowhere” in M.L.
for months. When he won the runner-up proved that he
was a ringer, and got the stake and div. Subsequently
the disqualified owner found that the objector’s own horse
was “ rung in ” also. He is now wondering whether there
ts any honor among gentlemen of the push as asserted.
* * * *
Australian bookmaker, Charley White who
died in London, the other week, had a funeral half-a-mile
long and “ floral tributes ” sufficient to decorate a whole
cemetery. That’s what comes of laying stiff ’uns for a
hvmg. Now that Charles himself is stiff and laid, his
familiar trade-motto, “ I am here,” will presumably be
carved on his tombstone. J

Equine demise : Haricot, winner of Melb Cup
’74. This race was a kind of fluke, Haricot bolting from
the field all the way round, just as Flying Buck did in the
first Champion. Many remember how the pigeons flew
up from the grand-stand with the news for the Werribee
homestead of the Chirnsides, who thenceforward took
loving care of the fitly-called son out of Saucepan by
Ladykirk, for such was the somewhat ambiguous name of
■ins sire.

Fact is, Persimmon couldn’t help winning the
Derby. Sydney D.T. of March 28 narrates how Mr. Grig-
son, of Brush tarm, shipped a small cargo of persimmons

* * * *
The Agri. trophies football-final at Sydney fur-
nished an unpleasant knock for the Wallaroos. Went-
worths had them in trouble throughout, and won by 9 to
0. It was an unexciting demonstration, and only one
man got hurt. It is quite possible that the losers will
make a much better stand in the challenge match against
Randwick this week, and that either of the teams will
down Wentworths later in the proposed charity-match.
Anything is liable to happen in Rugby.

R.C. writes: ‘‘ lie the current literary
endeavor to bring the R.CS. doctrine of Purgatory
‘in close touch ’ with the Theosophical theory of
re-incarnation. In Purgatory the soul, it is held,
can do nothing for itself ; in the theory of re-
incarnation the soul, by its own acts, can make
itself better or worse. Surely there is no possi-
bility of ‘ touch ’ between two such theories.”

In the matter of ornamental front-names,
there are signs amongst the ultraristoeracy of a
reaction towards the simplest; due, doubtless, to
the fact that appellations which used to be dis-
tinctly gilt-edged have passed, by way of London-
Journal literature,' so largely into the nomencla-
tui’eof the lick-spittle bourgeoisie, that ‘ ‘ before long,
begad, sir ! —for a plain Mary or Margaret ; for a
simple Jack, or Tom, or Harry —you’ll have to
come to us, sir—to us 1 ” But Blueblood feels that
there is balm in Gilead, after all—when, with
a sleeve - contained grin, it contemplates such
pleasing combinations as “Algernon Snooks,” or
“ Percy Plantagenet Gubbins,” or “ Gladys
Ermyntrade Blogg.”
The buyer of the unused half of a return-
ticket has not al ways the best of the deal. Re-
cently a Sydney man travelled from Melbourne to
Sydney, by steamer, on a doctor’s ticket bought
cheap. He was called up in the middle of the
night, when the boat was pitching dreadfully, by
three distracted women, to attend to a sick baby.
He prescribed for the infant at random ; but what
a pickle there would have been had it been—well,
another sort of case ! Confess you were not Dr.
• , and be dubbed an impostor and a spieler,
and be socially ostracised for the remainder of the
voyage, with the risk of arrest thrown in ? or
tackle the case and perform a horrid murder on
the trusting patient ? The agony, the fear of ex-
posure, the ever-present thought that some sailor
would break his leg and that you would be asked
to attend, discounted by a long way the apparent
cheapness of the ticket.
THE AUSTRALIAN GEM TRADE.
H. Newman, the well-known jeweller of 175
Elizabeth-street, Melbourne, is the largest buyer
of opal in Australia, and opal miners will find it
to their advantage to send their opal direct to
him. The firm has a reputation for straight deal-
ing extending over a period of 40 years, which is
a sufficient guarantee to consignors that they will
be honorably treated. H. Newman is also a
buyer of all other Australian gems.*
Dear Bulletin, — lie “H.F’s” sound de-
duction that, if the Cold-Tea assertions were true,
every alcohol-bibbing race would long ago have
petered-out, there are lots of other cases in which
hygienic theory can’t look fact in the face for a
moment. If washing the skin, for instance, is of
the fundamental importance claimed for it, how
account for the vigorous health of the Samoyed,
who never touches water, except through a capsize
of his lcayak? Also, if fresh air is the health-
essential it is cracked up to be, how do the Germans
—who religiously exclude it from their sleeping-
rooms—manage to remain the robustest race in
Europe ? Then there’s the opium-bogey. I have
lived where seven-eighths of the population habi-
tually and regularly used the drug, and yet there
wasn’t amongst them one opium-wreck for a dozen
drink-wrecks amongst ourselves. I don’t say these
discrepancies are inexplicable; but I can’t fix ’em
up.—A.M.

Mr. Button writes later: —“The Pills I had
from you were of great benefit to me and others.
In one case, when three hunters and trappers
were travelling about the ranges near my place on
the Rose River, one was taken very ill ; his mates
intended taking him in a waggon to Wangaratta
(about 60 miles) next morning to see a doctor. I
gave him a few of your Pills. lam pleased to
inform you that having to pass their camp next
day, I found him up and pegging-out his skins
and looking well. This speaks volumes for your
valuable Remedies.”

your Pills; since then I have used 4 boxes Special

of God, as well in health as I was 23 years ago.
I can with great confidence recommend your
Remedies to anyone suffering from the same com-
plaint. May you long be spared under God to

I remain, dear sir, yours very gratefully,
JOHN KEAY.

THE LAST OF SIX.”
Out in the backblocks the proud proprietor
of the “ Travelling Emporium of the Great
American Trading Association ” had drawn up his
waggon by the wayside to camp for the night, a
number of the ideal youths having deciphered
to the best of their collective ability, the gaudy
inscriptions on the tilt, were inspired with a laud-
able thirst for more extended geographical know-
ledge, and accordingly deputed their leader o
“ have a pitch with the bloke. Inspired y
muddiness of the waggon-wheels, the spokesman
ventured to “ ’spose that the road between here
and ‘ Ameriky ’is pretty bad. (< Waal, gravely
responded the interviewed, I reckon, .young
fellar, it’s a bit rough now and again m bad
weather, and there’s a tarnation big creek to
cross
A GRAND LITERARY INSTITUTION.
Cole’s Book Arcade now runs from the centre
of the block in Bourke-street to the centre of the
block in Collins-street, Melb., a distance of bbOtt.,
and contains the following 17 departments, mostly
literary and artistic: 1, New Books ; 2, Second-
hand Books ; 3, Stationery; 4, Music ; 5, 1 ancy
Goods ; 6, Toys ; 7, Perfumery; 8, Pictures ; 9,
Artists’ Materials ; 10, an Ornamental Exhibition ,
11, a Japanese Exhibition ; 12, a Wonderland ;
13, a Smiling Gallery; 14, a Childs Picture
Gallery ; 15, a Bargain Room ; 16, a Tea Room ;
17, a splendidly arranged new Wholesale Book
Department, containing half-a-million books. Ihe
mam walks of the establishment are half-a-mile
long.*
In one large city retail warehouse the
manager is engaged on the understanding that he
realises a certain profit every week—if the profit
isn’t there he gets ‘ ‘ the run. ” As business is none
too good there have consequently been tour
managers in about as many months. three ot
them lasted one week each, and the fourth, a per-
son of great business capacity, has filled m the
rest of the time. But he has had several narrow
escapes. One week the profit was a shilling or two
short, and it was within one minute of closing
time on the last night, but a tired girl-assistant
was fined ss. on a general charge of neglect
and incompetence, and that squared the accounts.
Next week, when the energetic proprietor took
stock, one pair of stockings was missing, and
as the value of them came off the profits there
seemed a chance that they would again fall just
below the limit. But the man in charge was
again equal to the occasion. He placed himselt
in a chair and solemnly stood the girl up ra front
of him. Then he informed her, in an awful voice,
that her iniquity had come to light, and that he
had at first intended to give her m custody tor
stealing the stockings, but on further consideration
hehad relented. Therefore he would merely deduct
them from her wages and discharge her there and
then, and presently a distressed female with a
balance of eighteenpence in her hand was cast into
the street.
At one large warehouse where discipline is un-
usually strict—discipline, as the term is under-
stood in the softgoods business, generally consists
in fiming everybody from 3d. up to 2s. bd. at short
intervals, and warning them in a deep voice not to
do it again—a large person in a tail-coat is kept
apparently for detective purposes. When the
periodical customer comes to say that something
she ordered was not sent, or something was sent
which she didn’t order, this individual backs ; her
into a chair and bows profoundly. “ Madam, he
says, “can you describe the young person who
served you ? ” She describes the young person,
and the inquisitor dives into the abysses of the
premises and returns leading the most likely-look-
ing young person by the wrist in custody, and
formally produces her for inspection. Madam,
he says in an awful voice, and with a withering
glance at the prisoner, “ is this the young person
who served you ? ” The proceedings are so awful
that it is quite obvious that if this is the young
person she will be immediately taken into the
cellar and executed, so if the customer has any
compassion in her she generally says it was fill 1 ’ 3 ®
a different young person, and goes away. But
sometimes there is a look of recognition m her eye,
and then the young person is generally fined on
suspicion.

|0 MINING COMPANIES, BATTERY OWNERS
AND OTHERS.
Increase your yields by Saving the Fine Gold
and Pyrites by the use of
Wynne and Tregurtha’s
JPatent Battery Plates.
This Invention, lately patented, revolutionises the ordi-
nary battery treatment of ores, for by its use copper-
plates, quicksilver, and chemicals are entirely dispensed
with The Wynne and Tregurtha Patent Battery Plates
are made of iron, cast with peculiarly-shaped ribs. They
are fitted in a wooden frame, and take the place of the
ordinary copper-plates and blanket tables. The tailings
on leaving the stamper boxes pass over the plates which,
from their construction, catch the fine gold P y ns ?
allowing the sand and water to flow away. When the
plates are fully charged with the concentrates they are
then tilted backwards by means of a lever, throwing the
contents into a trough underneath. In practical use
much better results have been obtained by these plates
than by the ordinary copper ones, the yield of gold being
increased by fully 10 per cent. Excellent results have
been achieved in the treatment of refractory ores, whore
the gold is associated with some foreign mineral, which
has proved deleterious to the mercury and causing the
loss of the gold. The plates are strong, simple in con-
struction, and easily handled, and can be obtained from

The . usual witticism of last Southern
Cross. (Melb. religious paper) gently dares thus :
A convivial elder fell off his horse down a bank,
bays another, ‘Faar are ye?’ ‘I dinna ken faar
1 be, but I in n 6 in hell, for there’s waiter.’ ’’
Ex-butcher Varley, Melbourne’s most tur-
bulent saint, in a recent issue of his weekly, prints
a sub-sermon on the godliness of capital punish-
■ n if jo ,should hanging and flaying be abol-
ished? asks the One Just Man. “Why should
we break a God-ordained law?” God’s laws are
strangely interpreted probably because God’s
lawyers, the parsons and evangelists, are self-
appointed and know less of the law than the
profits.

Victory!
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The clay was cold and dull and gray.
The long, white hospital-ward, studded with
cots, suggested, in that murky light, a catacomb •
filled with dead: with dead, chill, still, but yet
cognizant—dead awaiting judgment.
1,1 The mute, black-robed, and ever-flitting nurses
might have been attendant ghouls.
* * * *
Around one of the beds at the far end of
the ward a screen was drawn —that hospital screen,
on which might with grim appropriateness be
written the legend above the doors of Dante’s
Inferno.
“It is just nine o’clock. Were his an ordinary
case, I’d give him two hours to live, perhaps less ;
but not a minute longer.”
“ Do you think he will last longer, then, sir?”
queried the senior assistant of the chief surgeon.
“Well, poor wretch, he has something on his
mind— something that won’t let him die until he
tells it. And the power to tell it is apparently
gone.”
They crossed the ward, moved the screen a
little, and stood by the side of the bed, looking
down upon the occupant.
He who lay there was so old and deathly pallid,
so attenuated and helpless, that he might have
been deemed already dead, save for the strange
and lurid light blazing in his sunken eyes and the
awful look of expectancy on his shrivelled face-
such an appalling aspect of hope deferred as might
be worn by some damned soul, catching, through
the open gates of purgatory, a glimpse of the
portals of unattainable Paradise.
“Are you in much pain?” said the chief, in
business-like, but yet kindly tone.
The dying man replied only with a faint move-
ment of his eyebrows, which might have been
intended to mean— “No.”
The doctor glanced at the card at head of the
cot. “Religion, Church of England,” he mur-
mured. Then :
“ Would you like to see a clergyman ? ”
The unmistakeable shadow of contempt which
flitted athwart the sick man’s face was sufficient
negative.
“A lawyer or a magistrate? ”
He pursed his lips.
“No ! ” said the doctor. “ Then, is there any-
thing you’d like to tell me? You’re dying, you
know, my poor fellow. You can’t live two hours.”
A miracle happened.
“ I shan’t— bloomin’ —well—die—till —I know!
this man, who had been speechless for hours,
gasped.
“Know what?”
“ Garn ! ” said the patient with his lips, through
which, however, issued no further words, but only
the labored sobs of the outpaced, fainting runner.
* * * *
“ What a wretched physique, and yet what
marvellous tenacity of life ! ” remarked the assist-
ant as he and his chief paced softly up the ward.
“Yes,” said the other; “he’s a city product.
. He could never have walked three miles in any
one day of his life, or taken part in any exercise
requiring the slightest athleticism or endurance.
He nevfer had a musoular system, so to speak ; his
very bones have always been soft. ITe must have
been an old man from the day of his birth, and
he’s probably smoked and chewed some cwts. of
tobacoo, though his age, I see, is set down as 2b. ’
As the two medical men left the ward a
nurse drew near the dying man. He interrogated
her with terrible eyes.
“ No news yet,” she said.
Two hours passed. The man still lived. The
nurse approached his bed from time to time and
shook her head. Three hours ! He was alive.
His eyes still glittered with unholy fire. He was,
if possible, weaker than ever.
One o’clock ! The nurse, with much excitement
in her face, came swiftly to the bedside, bent
down, and whispered:
“In another hour. At two ! ”
* * * *
The chimes sounded from the. great tower
of Sydney Post-office. There was silence; then,
ponderously, the big clock struck :
One ! Two !
The dying man raised himself—actually raised
himself and sat up without assistance; he who
had stirred neither hand nor foot for weeks. It
was a resurrection—the resurrection of a long-dead
and galvanised corpse.
“ Shift—the —blanky screen ! ” he said ; “ lemme
—larf—at—that—big puddin’-’eaded Englishman
—next cot! ”
The nurse moved the screen. At that moment
there was a confused murmur at the entrance to
the ward, which swelled into a wild babble of
voices in which nurses and semi-moribund patients
alike joined.
Then, shrill and clear above all the others, a
voice—
“ The Australians have won the second test-
match / ”
The man who had waited raised himself—
higher—higher—until he almost stood in bed. He
transfixed with his unearthly eyes the burly Eng-
lishman in the next cot.
“ Yah, yah ! ” he said and fell over—stark and
dead. A light holy, sweet and tender, such as
might have been seen on the features of a saint,
seemed to irradiate his face —no longer the face of
an aged man, but of a wizened baby.
* * * ♦
“ Lived till two, did he ? ” said the doctor
next day.
“Yes, sir. He was only waiting to hear the
result of the test-match.”
“Very natural —quite proper ! What was he? ”
“The champion Sydney barracker, sir.”
J. EVISON.
The Maoriland papers tell a story about a
party of Druids who were driving home to Porirua
from a meeting of the Pahautanui Lodge or some-
thing, and, mistaking the light on an approaching
locomotive for a hotel, they drove furiously into
it in search of refreshment, and reduced their
horse and vehicle to a mass of ruin. The papers
call this a “curious accident,” and say it is “quite
unaccountable.” As if anything that could
happen to a party of Druids coming home from a
lodge is unaccountable ! The Bulletin doesn’t
know much about Druids, but it once attended a
lodge of Free Masons or Enslaved Bricklayers, or
something of the sort, and after it had prevented
three of them harnessing a cow to their buggy by
mistake for a horse, it met one brother on the way
home fighting six rounds with a telegraph-post,
and another standing undressed in the middle of
the road and polishing his boots with a lump of
grass. Still another brother was missing for two
days afterwards, and then he was found sitting on
the steps of the Gr.P.O., covered with soot and
eating a banana,.
The Whale-Cure for Rheumatism*
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I WAS helpless with rheumatism, and had tried
every cure unavailingly. Some time ago a whale
was caught near Bega and I was advised to try
the whale-cure for rheumatism—take a bath in the
whale ! Of course Johnson and I—Johnson ached
more than I did —laughed at the idea ! Another
day another whale was killed, and I was worse.
Again the whale-cure was suggested; this time
I began to think, but didn’t face it. A week
after the third whale came in, and I was getting
worse still; so we decided to go over next morn-
ing—and get in. , . „
The morning came; Captain Young was
there in charge of the steam-whaler Jenny
Lind, and kindly consented to run us across
the bay, about four miles, to the trymg-out
works at Kiah. I was lifted into the boat,
for I could hardly move. Johnson had gone
over earlier. We got over safely, crossed the bar,
rounded a point to the left, and there lay two
great whales stripped of the blubber and awaiting
the rising of the tide to be towed away. But,
great Caesar, the smell!
We got ashore, and I was carried to a hut near
the trying-down works. There, on the top of the
biggest whale, trying to cut a rib out, was my
fellow-sufferer, Johnson; and to make the awful
smell less horrible (as if that was possible) he was
singing at the top of his voice. When the rib
was removed, after being divested of all clothes
but a singlet and a borrowed pair of trousers, I
was carried up and placed in the hole cut in the
flesh. My instructions were to work myself down
into the flesh as far as I could, and there stand
until I felt too weak to put up with any more
pressure. Then I was to be dragged out and
dressed warmly and sent home.
Well, I did work down until I imagined every
moment I was going right under. You see, there
was room for two more men to stand beneath me,
the whale being about 17 or 18ft. deep; but the
pressure held me, and when I got down to my
arm-pits I held on to the next-door rib and thought
of the smell. I stood there for an hour and 20
minutes, and just near me was my friend Johnson,
who really seemed to enjoy the situation and kept
shouting smart remarks across the rib that
separated us. But for his company, I could not
have stood it ten minutes.
The smell and heat were hardly bearable. The
whale had been dead about forty hours, and had
started to decompose, and the whole time we were
sitting and standing there great blasts of gas and
horrible bubbles would gush out around us and
make our hair stand on end. The heat, as near as
I could guess, was about llOdeg. ; the smell aver-
aged between a million and a-half and two million
degrees. They say that the older a whale is the
more he smells, and the longer he is dead the
worse he smells. Apparently this whale dated
back some 500 years 8.C., and died in its infancy.
But afterwards ! They dragged me out of the
whale’s side, and I ran down his tail on to the
the shore and up to where my clothes were ! The
effect was wonderful. They rubbed me down,
dressed me and put several coats around me, and
put me in the boat; then we returned to Eden.
That evening I found where the pain had been
worst in my right side a patch had turned quite
black, and the nails on my right hand and foot
were as black as ink, but the rheumatism was all
gone—not a twinge ! They got me a glass of rum
to get a good perspiration up, but the rum refused
to go below my head; the smell was too strong
for it. . i
After four days I walked up town, and everyone
I met shied at me—a leper copld not have been
avoided more'discreetly. Girls that I knew cut
me dead ; men whom I considered true brothers
held their notes and bolted; sometimes* a man
would gallop past, and, if on a fast horse, would say
“Good-day!” and vanish. Of course, being a
country fellow, and sensitive, I felt this—never
dreaming the true cause. At last I met Johnson
and thcii it began tO ; daWn On me. Did I smell
like he did? Then I caught his eye, and, sure
enough, there was the same question lurking there.
Then I knew. .
For exactly twelve months the rheumatism left
me ; then it came back again as bad as ever. The
smell has never left me : that dead whale, haunts
me still.
TRAVELLER.
Prof. Vincent, of the Royal Geographical
Society, has been investigating the mosquito
question. To the Gulf of Mexico and Greenland
the professor awards the palm for producing the
most ferocious of mosquitoes. Has he sampled
the grey-backs that haunt the mangrove swamps
at the mouths of Queensland riVers ?
Joseph Chamberlain and Miss Helen
Gould are the two leading orchid fanciers and pur-
chasers in Europe. South America is the great
field for orchids, but there’s many an orchid-
hunter who would make a handsome thing by
turning his attention to the scrubs of North
Queensland, where gorgeous specimens are found
in abundance.
Gov. M‘Gregor remarks that he found the
women of Western New Guinea remarkably de-
void of personal attractions—man being un-
doubtedly the superior animal in that locality.
The only personal adornment permitted the
females consists of four cicatrical marks extending
from breast to breast. The principal chief, clad
in blue trousers and white shirt, paid a visit to
the Governor. The next visitor was similarly
arrayed, and it was finally discovered that , the
one shirt did duty for the whole band of callers
in succession being transferred from one to
the other as the dusky visitants went and came.
Gov. M‘Gregor notices with disapprobation the
disposition on the part of New Guinea females to
change the simple native petticoat for calico, and
is of opinion that the native garment is much
cleaner and more graceful. He notices also that
native houses become worse built as civilisation
advances. ... Native teacher at Turituri wanted
the children to come to school dressed in European
clothes. The parents were quite willing if he
would only provide the' clothes. To this the
teacher demurred, wherefore a deadlock en-
sued and the young Papuans continued to
roam about naked, ignorant, healthy, and happy.
The life of a native teacher in New Guinea is by
no means devoid of interest. The other day a
native teacher (minus pupils) turned his attention
to pig-shooting. Invading the premises of his
next-door neighbor, he slaughtered porkers twain ;
three more fell victims to his fury out in the road,
and a sixth he slaughtered inside his own fence.
His neighbors assembled with the laudable intent
of treating the Man of God as he had treated the
pigs. The arrival of the Governor’s launch inter-
rupted the circus, and the teacher was condemned
to pay the costs of his pork-hunting. But, then,
what could he do (pleaded the defendant), the
people would not come to school.”


The will of Lady Burton, widow of the
explorer, devotes adequate funds for 3000 masses
to be said for her soul by a French provincial
priest. She also capitalises enough for a daily
mass in a Paris church for all time! Further,
the Bishop of Southwark receives enough t 0
ensure five anniversary masses on the days
respectively of her birth, marriage and death, and
Gapt. Burton’s birth and death. All this makes
a considerable hole in the <£12,000 she leaves.
Also, she provides that, after death, she is to be
pierced through the heart with a needle. Her
body is to be embalmed, and is to be laid beside
Burton’s, in the tomb above ground, but “in case
of a revolution in England, which may lead to
desecration of the cemeteries,” the bodies are to be
removed to a vault underground.
Dear Bulletin, —During the Public Ser-
vice Board discussion in N.S.W. Parlt., it was
said that teachers should “get more pay than
laborer-s. ” Why? It is about time this comparison
was jumped on. If a teacher saw navvies on rail-
way works slinging muck into waggons, for four
hours at a stretch, he would probably be content.
The writer remembers one navvy, a bit out of
fettle (he had been a month on the road), going
into a cutting with a seasoned gang. He got
through that day all right; the next three he spent
on his back, and he was a six-footer, too. The
clerical man has no night-shifts, with poor ventila-
lation, or tent-living on bad water, and gets as
much as the other with these thrown in. How
long does it take the sun and wet and foul air to
break up the ordinary laborer ? —Muscle.

In Substitution ,
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“ Remember, man, for every sheep miss-
ing at muster, a lash ! There are no dogs about
here save your own, and nothing but neglect can
occasion loss. So look out, my man.” And rosy-
cheeked, bullet-headed Captain Scone rode away,
leaving David Fletcher, 39-176 per ship Hercules,
his newly-assigned shepherd, in charge of 1600
wethers in the range country of his estate of Avon
Dale.
A weakling of the weaklings was Fletcher:
emotional, impulsive, irrational; meaning well
ever, and ever acting ill. He meant to do his duty
in this new sphere of life to which Providence—so
he termed the laws of his own ill-deeds—had
called him. And so, after the manner of his
kind, he didn’t do it —he only dreamed of doing
it, and frittered away in reverie the energy and
time which would have won him his master’s ap-
proval, and it happened that three months later he
was short a hundred and forty sheep.
One hundred and forty lashes ! Fourteen
tallies of ten each ! For Captain Scone, not un-
kind, was just no less to himself than others.
Justice would assuredly demand,' then, the pay-
mentgfer his loss. And, under the circumstances,
therecwas but one method of payment. Lacking
penc ■, there was but Fletcher’s carcase.
Suffering already in anticipation the pains of a
thousand lashes, Fletcher sped from his hut across
the valley to his one neighbor—another of Scone’s
shepherds. Canton was, however, more experi-
enced in many ways. He was a transport of ten
years’ standing, he had felt the curl and swirl of
the lash on two different days, and he was indif-
ferent as to what happened for good or evil.
“ What shall I do ! ” moaned Fletcher, thus
expressing his pain, but scarce expecting an
answer.
Canton replied. “Do ? Take your flog-gin’, o’
course.”
“ But ’twill kill me—kill me ! One-forty—one-
forty ! ”
“Which—body or soul?” derisively questioned
the other. Then he answered himself : “Fellows
like you have no soul. O’ course ’tis your body—
your poor skin as you’re afraid of. Now, look
here ! If you like you can take one-forty of my
sheep and I’ll be short that number and take the
floggin’.”
The craven Fletcher eagerly accepted the offer :
“Oh, will you? How good! And when can I
drive your sheep over?”
‘ ‘ Now ! ” There was a measureless contempt in
the monosyllable.
* * * «
A week later the flocks were mustered, and
Canton’s was short by seven-score.
To the nearest township which boasted of a
triangles, and where the bench could be depended
upon to ratify such an arrangement between
master and man as appraised a sheep at a lash,
went, in due course, Canton. In due course he
pleaded guilty ; in due course he was flogged.
Later, he encountered the fellow for whom he
had suffered. Fletcher, aglow with what he
thought gratitude, but which was nothing save
pleasure at the wholeness of his own skin, thanked
him, and then timorously asked him a question.
“ What did I do it for ? God knows ! I don’t!”
PRICE WARONG.
A Rope’s End.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
SOON after the finding of gold in New Guinea by
Andrew Goldie’s party first drew attention to that
mysterious island, the untamed insular Myall, as
free from the blighting influence of our civilisa-
tion as when his progenitor first sighted the
Endeavor’s topsails, peering down from his rugged
granite-buttressed heights saw the steam-lighter
Youra lying snugly moored at the head of the
narrow cliff-girt inlet deeply indenting the northern
shore of lonely Lizard Island.
Bound for the new “ El-Dorado ” on a roving
commission (destined to terminate in the murder
of all hands save the narrator by the cannibals of
Utean), we had sailed from Cairns, some fortnight
previously, in so unseaworthy a condition that we
had to call in at Cooktown to refit. While there
our only deck-hand had deserted, and as the over-
laden and leaky scow was little calculated to in-
spire Jack’s confidence, we were unable to induce
any white seamen to sign articles, the only crew
obtainable being a couple of new-chum Chinese
whose nautical knowledge was strictly limited to
their experiences on the passage from the Flowery
Land.
At this time we were only five hands, all told ;
the balance of the ship’s company consisting of
I , skipper and owner, a young ex-naval middy,
recklessly courageous, but necessarily a poor and
inexperienced seaman ; and A , who acted as
engineer—a thorough “ shell-back ” from scalp to
toe-nails, who confessed to every marine employ-
ment short of piracy, but whose technical attain-
ments barely qualified him to boil a billy. And
then there was the writer, who occupied a position
too anomalous for special designation.
We had touched at this out-of-the-way island (a
few years later to acquire painful notoriety as the
opening scene of the tragedy in which the heroic
Mrs. Watson played such a pitiable part) in order
to cut firewood to eke out our scanty supply of
coals, and, on coming to anchor, the skipper had
landed in our only boat, a fine, new four-oared
“ whaler,” which, being too long to take aboard,
we had in tow.
Landing on the pretty little beach, at the head
of the sheltered cove, I- had left the China-
men to fill the breakers at a spring and cut the
stunted mangrove, the only wood favorable, and
with characteristic rashness had set out alone to
explore the island, hoping, as he said, to fall in
with some of the natives and perhaps induce one
or two of the young “ bucks ” to come with us and
recruit our unique crew.
» ♦ • *
Later in the afternoon, while finishing in the
deckhouse-cabin a watercolor sketch of rugged
scenery, I was startled by a shout from A—-,
busy forward patching the forestaysail, who called
my attention to the fact that the “Chows” had not
made fast the boat. It had floated off the beach
by the rising tide, and was drifting rapidly past us,
impelled by the fresh southerly breeze.
Knowing that my companion, whose pluck was
beyond question, could not swim a stroke, I had
no alternative but to take to the water in pursuit
without more delay than was necessary to detach
my revolver-pouch from my belt. Fortunately I
was barefooted and, of course, lightly clad.
Always a fair long-distance swimmer, the task
did not look difficult, and I had little doubt of
speedily overhauling the derelict if unmolested by
sharks. Although, however, it had certainly not
got a start of more than 100 yards when I plunged
oYer the low sid o of the vessel, the empty boat
drifting broadside-on offered so much surface to
the wind and had such a slight hold of the water
that it slipped over the toppling wavelets at a rate
that compelled me to swim my fastest in order to
make any material gain.
Certainly at first the gap was rapidly reduced,
the fear of sharks being quite as powerful an in-
centive to “put it all on” as the most alluring
trophy. But the strain was too severe to last, and
I soon found it necessary to husband my failing
strength by easing up, and frequent changes of
stroke and position.
Still about a third of the distance that had
separated me from the object of my pursuit re-
mained unbridged, when I found that the little
harbor was left behind and that every stroke
carried me further from terra firma. I also per-
ceived to my dismay that in the open water, less
sheltered by the land, the speed of the boat’s
drift had so increased that only by exerting myself
in such a manner as threatened speedy exhaustion
could I gain an inch upon the boat. In fact
I was not very sure that I could gain at all. I re-
solved to give up the pursuit while there was yet
time to return, so, turning in despair, I struck out
for the nearest rocky headland.
The sea was now running, however, with a nasty
curl, which while swimming with it I had not
much noticed, and this made progress so uncertain
and distressing that in time the conviction was
forced upon me that my only chance after all was
to reach the boat, now much further to seaward
than when I had first started.
* * * *
Unspeakably depressed, I again turned my back
upon the land with but faint hopes of success,
True it was some relief to again swim with the
run of the sea, but I was already much spent; it
seemed only a question of time when I should
experience those delicious sensations of drowning
so often recounted by resuscitated persons, but
which, it is to be confessed, I recalled with doubt
and certainly without desire. Still I managed to
struggle on, the sensation of fatigue rather wear-
ing away than otherwise, and the motions of my
limbs becoming more mechanical, while an almost
pleasing lassitude crept over me.
In certain phases of danger and nervous
excitement the minutes seem ages; in others,
when physical exhaustion numbs the senses,
the reverse is rather the case. The range
of a swimmer’s vision is but limited, yet every
now and again as I rose on a wave-crest I caught
sight of the drifting boat still about the same dis-
tance ahead; the only other incident in the
struggle being the occasional visit of a booby,
which seemed to regard me with a fearless
curiosity as it skimmed in its wheeling flight close
to my upturned face.
M hether I should ever have succeeded in gain-
ing the boat is more than doubtful had not the
monsoon, as usual, begun to decline with the
setting sun, now little above the distant coast-line,
Noticing this, with renewed hope I pulled myself
together for a final effort, and soon saw with satis-
faction that I was steadily gaining ; but, with the
strengthening hope of safety, the fear of sharks,
which during the latter part of my long swim had
hardly seemed to trouble me, so increased as almost
to paralyse my heart: for the last few yards I
struggled along in a state of indescribable trepida-
tion, which culminated m an agony of horror as,
just on getting within arm’s-length of safety, I
felt something rough glide along my right leg.
With a supreme effort, which must otherwise have
been my last, I frantically clutched the gunwale,
heeling the empty boat nearly to the water’s edge;
how I managed to get in I know not, unless fear
lent me the requisite strength.
* * * *
’ When I recovered consciousness I found myself
lymg on the bottom boards of the whaler, where
I must have lain prostrated with fatigue and
fright for some considerable time, the sun being
now set and the island fading away far to the
southward. In those days, however, I was young
and hardy, and the nature of my occupations had
so indurated nerve and sinew that I soon pulled
myself together and set to work to get sail on the
boat.
There was still a nice little air, quite enough for
a boat in such light trim. She was a smart, well-
rigged little craft and weatherly ; so by the time,
night had fallen we were footing it merrily tc
windward. Steering by the “ Cross,” in about an
hour’s time I picked up the Voura’s mast-head
light which A had thoughtfully lit as a beacon,
and sometime before midnight succeeded in gain-
ing the anchorage without further mishap.
Truth compels me to state that the ferocious
shark whose maw I had so narrowly escaped,
turned out to be merely the trailing painter
of. the boat, which had grazed my shin in swim-
ming over it.

“ My Dear Mr. BONNINGTON.
